Greg Hudson, the linebackers coach at Florida State, has accepted the position of defensive coordinator at Purdue. This follows the news that Tim Tibesar would not be retained as Purdue's defensive coordinator. Florida State's defense, under Hudson's guidance, was very effective this season. In two games the author watched closely, against Miami and Northern Illinois, the defense significantly stifled the opposing offenses. Hudson previously served as the defensive coordinator at East Carolina from 2005 to 2009, leading the Pirates to a successful period with two Conference-USA championships and a strong defense. Hudson has also held linebackers coaching positions at Cincinnati, Minnesota, and Florida State. He played at Notre Dame for two seasons. This past season, Florida State had a 12-2 record, won the ACC, and the Orange Bowl. Their defense was ranked sixth nationally, allowing an average of 14.7 points per game. They held six opponents to 10 points or fewer. Hudson coached standout linebackers Christian Jones and Telvin Smith at Florida State. Jones is considered one of the top linebackers in the country and may enter the NFL draft early. The author believes Hudson can help Purdue improve their linebacker position, which has struggled since the departure of Niko Koutouvides and Landon Johnson a decade ago.
Mamoru Samuragochi, a 50-year-old Japanese musician, was widely admired for his compositions featured in popular video games and the routine of a top figure skater for the upcoming Sochi Olympics. His deafness garnered him comparisons to Beethoven. However, it was revealed that his greatest creation was a deception. On Thursday, Japan discovered that Samuragochi had orchestrated a complex hoax. Someone else secretly composed his most renowned works, and he may have even fabricated his hearing impairment. The nation, deeply devoted to Western classical music, responded with a mixture of disappointment, anger, and even legal threats following Samuragochi's admission that he had employed a ghostwriter since the 1990s to compose most of his music. The shock deepened when the ghostwriter came forward, accusing Samuragochi of feigning deafness to garner public sympathy and cultivate the Beethoven image. The scandal erupted on Wednesday when Samuragochi publicly confessed that someone else had written his most celebrated pieces, including Symphony No. 1 “Hiroshima,” a classical music sensation in Japan that commemorates the 1945 atomic bombing of his hometown; the theme music for the video games Resident Evil and Onimusha; and Sonatina for Violin, scheduled to be performed by Japanese Olympic figure skater Daisuke Takahashi in Sochi.
Some feminist academics oppose plans to criminalize street harassment in France, arguing that it could unfairly target men of migrant backgrounds. They contend that these men already face disproportionate police violence and oppression and that focusing on training rather than criminalization would be more effective. The academics also criticize the proposed law's limited scope, as it only addresses street harassment, while women can experience abuse in other settings like homes and workplaces. They warn that criminalizing street harassment might inadvertently conceal abuse occurring in wealthier areas or corporate offices. The equality minister, Marlène Schiappa, explained that the new law aims to address situations like a man persistently asking a woman for her phone number on public transportation, making her feel harassed. This initiative follows surveys highlighting the prevalence of street harassment in France. A 2015 study by the High Council for Equality between Women and Men found that 100% of women in migrant-heavy areas had experienced sexual harassment or assault.
Cisco has issued a security alert stating that several of its Adaptive Security Appliance (ASA) products are vulnerable to a remote code execution flaw. The vulnerability stems from an issue in how the ASA products handle fragmented Internet Key Exchange (IKE) payloads. Cisco's implementation of the fragmentation protocol contains a bounds-checking error. This flaw allows an attacker to overflow a heap buffer with malicious data, leading to remote code execution. The affected products include the ASA 5500, ASA 5500-X, ASA Services Module for Catalyst 6500 switches and 7600 Series routers, the ASA 1000V Cloud Firewall, the Adaptive Security Virtual Appliance, the Firepower 9300 ASA module, and the ISA 3000. Both IPv4 and IPv6 traffic can exploit this vulnerability. The vulnerability arises because while the fragment length is 16 bits, Cisco limits its queues to 8 bits. Attackers can exploit this by creating a reassembly queue with a fragment length less than the default 8-byte fragment header size, causing an underflow during reassembly. This can force a system reload. However, more sophisticated attacks can leverage this underflow to inject attacker code into a writable memory location, granting the attacker control of the device. Cisco has released patches for affected software versions.
Six friends are at a pub. The first friend complains their glass is half empty and wants another drink. The second friend says their glass is half full and they're fine. The third friend mentions a long line at the bar and suggests waiting. The fourth friend proposes going to the bar now and ordering two rounds to get drunk. The fifth friend leaves, not wanting to argue, wait, or drink excessively. Meanwhile, the sixth friend drinks everyone's drinks while they discuss what to do. The story suggests that pessimists always want more, optimists are annoyingly content, realists are annoying, cynics create drama, quitters are irrelevant, and opportunists have all the fun. So, the next time you go out for a drink, remember to have more fun and argue less, or someone else will take advantage of you.

Malaysian police arrested four individuals suspected of terrorism between August 2nd and September 17th. Three of the suspects, who were foreigners, have since been deported. The arrested individuals include a Bangladeshi restaurant owner suspected of smuggling weapons for international terror groups. Interpol had issued a "Red Notice" for him. A Nepali citizen who managed an entertainment outlet was also arrested for allegedly falsifying travel documents for members of international terror groups. A Moroccan man was arrested on suspicion of being an Islamic State member. Turkish authorities previously detained him for attempting to smuggle into Syria. The fourth suspect, a Malaysian, worked as a personal chauffeur for a businessman. He is believed to be connected to Muhammad Wanndy Mohamad Jedy, who police allege has been actively promoting Islamic State activities on his Facebook page.
A new report from Fidelity Investments and Stanford Center on Longevity, based on a survey of 9,000 employees, reveals that one in nine baby boomer parents have welcomed their adult children back home within the last year. While adult children benefit from reduced expenses on rent and household goods, the parents report increased stress, unhappiness, and less leisure time. Over three-quarters of parents also experience higher expenses due to their children's return. This trend extends to older millennials and Generation Xers, who are more likely to live with their parents now compared to 1999. Fidelity's Katie Taylor emphasizes the importance of financial realism when assisting children in need. The number of American adults living with their parents and grandparents has reached a record high, with 19% of the population, or nearly 61 million people, residing in multigenerational households. However, not all millennials are motivated solely by financial reasons. A significant portion of those living at home are doing so due to health issues or caregiving responsibilities. Furthermore, many millennials plan to financially support their parents in the future, including assisting with retirement savings.

Dozens of unpublished photographs from the Second Boer War depicting battle scenes have been discovered in a Derbyshire home. The collection, including medals and badges, will be auctioned in late July. Auctioneer Charles Hanson described the find as "a time capsule that sheds new light on the war." The photographs were taken by Robert Oliver, a survivor of the 1899-1902 conflict. He received the Queen's Medal for South Africa for his service in Cape Colony, the Orange Free State, and the Transvaal in 1901 and 1902. Hanson noted that the "graphic images record the harsh reality and true horror of war - something the Victorian press did not portray at the time, as the British Empire was considered undefeatable." The photos include images of war dead, a British serviceman's funeral, and a Boer War dog. The collection also features two pairs of kid gloves, spurs, an ammunition bandolier, caps and hats, a South African feathered headdress, and a cartridge belt. Hanson added that Oliver "was quite a rogue in his youth. At 16, he ran away, ending up on a ship to Canada where he worked as a lumberjack. He later joined Staffordshire Police and was considered… funny but firm and strict." He also owned and operated the Devonshire Pub in Hartington, Derbyshire. The sale will take place on July 25th.

An international court ruled in favor of Slovenia in a long-standing maritime dispute with Croatia, granting Slovenia direct access to international waters in the Adriatic Sea. While the Permanent Court of Arbitration's decision is final and binding, Croatia, which withdrew from the proceedings in 2015, stated it would disregard the ruling. The court determined that Slovenia should have "uninterrupted access" to the sea it shares with Croatia. Croatian Prime Minister Andrej Plenkovic stated that the decision was not legally binding and that Croatia had no intention of implementing it. He expressed openness to bilateral dialogue with Slovenia. Slovenia's government welcomed the ruling, calling it "definitive and legally binding." Slovenian Prime Minister Miro Cerar stated that Slovenia would continue its good cooperation with Croatia and would not take any actions to worsen their relations. The dispute, centered on the bay of Piran, arose after both countries declared independence from Yugoslavia in 1991. The issue delayed Croatia's accession to the EU for many years. Only after both parties agreed to arbitration was Zagreb granted entry to the bloc in 2013. The court ruled that Slovenia would have the majority of the Piran bay area recognized as its territorial waters. Additionally, the tribunal established a corridor through Croatian waters to provide Slovenia with direct access to international waters. Croatia withdrew from the arbitration process in 2015 after a leaked tape revealed a Slovenian judge improperly exchanging confidential information with the Ljubljana government. The judge resigned, and the court continued the case, asserting that the incident did not compromise the court's ability to reach a final verdict. Slovenia maintains that Croatia should respect the verdict, while Croatia insists the issue should be resolved bilaterally. The EU has viewed the arbitration between Croatia and Slovenia as a potential model for handling other border disputes among Balkan EU hopefuls. Croatia's unilateral withdrawal raises concerns about the success of future arbitrations and whether promises made to secure EU accession will be honored after a state joins the union.
REC Silicon announced it will not meet its original production and sales targets for the third quarter of 2016 due to the ongoing US-China trade war and a decline in the photovoltaic market. The company will operate at approximately 50% capacity at its Moses Lake facility by October 1st, 2016, and production will remain at lower levels until market conditions improve. REC Silicon now projects Q3 polysilicon sales (excluding fines and powders) to be 1,800 tonnes, resulting in revenues between USD 45 million and USD 50 million for the July-September period. This represents a decrease from the previous quarter's USD 71.1 million due to lower Fluidised Bed Reactor (FBR) sales. Total Q3 polysilicon production is forecast at 3,900 tonnes, falling short of the initial guidance of 4,490 tonnes. FBR production is estimated at 3,300 tonnes, compared to the previous projection of 3,830 tonnes. The company is also in discussions with its joint venture partner to delay its second and third capital contributions, totaling USD 169 million, for the construction of a FBR polysilicon plant in Yulin, China, beyond 2018. REC Silicon stated that these contributions cannot be made until the trade war is resolved.
A school minister at Phillips Exeter Academy asked a male student accused of sexual misconduct to bake bread for his alleged victim as a form of penance. This unusual arrangement, reported by The Boston Globe's Spotlight team, comes as New England's elite boarding schools face scrutiny over their handling of sexual misconduct. In May, The Globe reported that an award-winning teacher and administrator at Phillips Exeter admitted to sexual misconduct decades earlier, but the school did not disclose this to students or alumni. Last fall, 17-year-old student Michaella Henry told administrators that a male student had touched her inappropriately, despite her repeated protests. Instead of involving police, school minister Rev. Robert Thompson brought the alleged perpetrator, Chukwudi "Chudi" Ikpeazu, and Henry together to resolve the issue. The result was an agreement that Ikpeazu would bake and deliver a weekly loaf of "monkey bread" to Henry. At the minister's suggestion, Ikpeazu also agreed to bake bread for Henry as a "penance" for the rest of the year. Rev. Thompson praised Henry for accepting the arrangement, stating that she gave Ikpeazu an opportunity to express regret and take responsibility. However, the agreement increased Henry's stress as she was required to meet with Ikpeazu weekly. She eventually reported the incident to police in the spring, and Ikpeazu is scheduled to be arraigned on a misdemeanor charge of sexual assault in August.
Graduates of the class of 2015 left college with an average of $30,000 in student loan debt, a 4 percent increase from the previous year, according to a report released by The Institute for College Access and Success (TICAS). TICAS president Lauren Asher stated the need for more affordable college and less burdensome debt for students and families. California's average college debt was $22,000, the third lowest nationally, but it is rising at a similar rate to the national average. National University had the highest average debt in California at $53,000. Debt at University of California campuses ranged from $17,869 at UC Berkeley to $22,580 at UC Santa Cruz. At California State University campuses, debt among graduates ranged from $10,872 at Fresno State to $23,973 at Humboldt State. The report acknowledges a significant gap as it does not include data from for-profit colleges, which often do not disclose student debt levels. TICAS associate research director Diane Cheng noted that actual state averages may be higher because schools with high debt levels can choose not to provide data, and reporting schools may not be aware of all private loans taken by their students. Using U.S. Department of Education data, the institute found that in 2012, student debt for graduates of for-profit colleges was approximately $40,000. The report recommends that states prioritize financial need when awarding publicly funded college grants, inform students about their loan options to help them make informed decisions, and require colleges to inform students about available federal aid before approving private loans, similar to California's policy.

In a letter released Friday, the Department of Justice (DOJ) urged the Census Bureau to include a citizenship question on the 2020 census forms. DOJ lawyer Arthur Gary wrote to Census Bureau official Dr. Ron Jarmin, arguing that the question is necessary to effectively enforce the Voting Rights Act. The DOJ stated that accurate data on the citizen voting-age population is crucial for investigating potential voting rights violations. The Census Bureau confirmed receipt of the letter, stating that any potential question undergoes a standard review process. This request follows reports suggesting the Trump administration might include an immigration status question in the 2020 census. Critics fear such a question could discourage participation, while supporters believe it would safeguard election integrity.
Fulham Football Club is pleased to announce the signing of defensive midfielder Mickael Tavares on a short-term contract. The 29-year-old previously worked with Fulham manager Martin Jol at Hamburger SV during the 2008/09 Bundesliga season. Tavares has earned 25 caps for the Senegal national team. Born in Villeneuve-Saint-Georges, France, Tavares began his professional career at SC Abbeville, scoring nine goals in 26 games during the 2002/03 season. He subsequently played for FC Nantes, FC Tours, and Slavia Prague in the Czech Republic, where he participated in the Champions League. After two seasons in Prague, he joined Jol's Hamburg in 2009. During his three years with the German club, he was loaned to FC Nuremberg and Middlesbrough. He made 12 appearances for Middlesbrough in the Championship during the 2010/11 season before returning to Hamburg. Tavares became a free agent after leaving Hamburg in the summer.

Bill Gates, co-founder of Microsoft and philanthropist, announced on Sunday that the Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation will increase its funding to eradicate malaria. This decision is part of Gates' broader strategy to combat tropical diseases. The foundation will raise its malaria budget by 30 percent, allocating over $200 million annually, in addition to its existing contributions to the Global Fund to Fight AIDS, Tuberculosis and Malaria. The Gates Foundation will commit over $500 million this year to fight various diseases, including pneumonia, diarrhea, and parasitic diseases, in low-income countries. Gates expressed his belief that malaria can be eradicated during his lifetime. The foundation's plans include developing a drug or vaccine to eliminate malaria in asymptomatic carriers. The foundation's efforts to eradicate malaria, which claims over 600,000 lives annually, also include a $156 million grant to the PATH Malaria Vaccine Initiative (MVI) to develop new vaccines that interrupt the transmission cycle of the malarial parasite. Steve Davis, PATH's president and CEO, stated that this grant will support their strategy to control, eliminate, and ultimately eradicate malaria. In addition to malaria, the Gates Foundation pledged $50 million for research on Ebola treatments, tests, and vaccines. Gates emphasized the need for a larger effort to contain the Ebola outbreak, which has claimed approximately 5,000 lives in eight countries this year. He warned that a more transmissible epidemic could emerge in the coming decades and urged countries to strengthen their healthcare systems and invest in new drugs and vaccines.

While NFC is commonly used for digital wallets and device pairing, it can also generate a small amount of power. This power is insufficient for everyday devices but is enough to run a small E Ink display. This technology, discovered on the NFC Brief site, has practical applications. E Ink displays only consume power when refreshing, making them ideal for displaying static information wirelessly. For instance, an NFC-enabled smartphone could transfer data and power to a special E Ink display, showcasing important information. A video demonstrating this concept is available. The NFC tag, developed by Intel Labs, the University of Washington, and the University of Massachusetts Amherst, showcases the potential of this technology. Although no commercial products currently exist, the concept and demonstration are promising. Imagine companion displays in cars, on desks, or even integrated into laptop bags, providing static but useful information without requiring additional power.
A new study suggests a link between childhood obesity and lower math scores. Researchers analyzed data from 6,250 children tracked from kindergarten to fifth grade. They found that children who were persistently obese throughout elementary school performed worse on math tests by first grade compared to children who were never obese. Girls who became obese later in the study also experienced a temporary decline in math skills. Obesity negatively impacted children beyond academics. Girls who were persistently obese had fewer social and interpersonal skills. Obese children also reported feeling sadder, lonelier, and more anxious, which could have contributed to their math performance. While the study highlights a correlation, it's unclear whether obesity causes these problems or if other factors contribute. Experts suggest a complex interplay between obesity, social skills, emotional well-being, and academic performance.
Thousands of rats have invaded villages in southern Myanmar, leading authorities to offer residents 4 US cents for each rat they kill. Villagers are using tools like sticks, slingshots, and rocks to combat the infestation and have reportedly killed over 4,000 rats. While concerns exist about potential diseases spread by the rats, locals also worry that their presence could signal other disasters. Regional MP Phyo Zaw Shwe told AFP that people believe these animals can predict bad weather, leading to fears of floods or earthquakes. This belief may have some basis, as a study by Japanese scientists suggests rats might be sensitive to electromagnetic waves that occur before earthquakes. Although the rats' bodies have been sent for testing, no diseases have been detected so far. Local officials attribute the infestation to heavy rains in Myanmar's Ayeyarwady region, which have caused widespread food consumption by the rats. This is not the first time a rat plague has threatened villages in Myanmar. In 2008, thousands of people in Chin State faced starvation after rats consumed bamboo fruits and food crops. This phenomenon occurs approximately every 50 years. During the last outbreak in the 1950s, an estimated 15,000 people died from starvation.
At least 11 people, including Somali government soldiers and al-Shabaab fighters, were killed in an attack on an army base in Bulo-gadud, Somalia, on Sunday. Colonel Mukhtar Abdi Mohamed of Jubaland state reported that four soldiers and seven al-Shabaab militants were killed, with more than 10 people from both sides wounded. He said an al-Shabaab suicide car bomb detonated at the base, followed by intense fighting. General Ismail Sahardid of the Somali National Army stated that they killed over 20 al-Shabaab militants during the engagement. An eyewitness, speaking anonymously, described a large explosion followed by two hours of heavy fighting. Al-Shabaab claimed responsibility for the attack, alleging they killed over 26 government soldiers and captured three military vehicles. Bulo-gudud is located 30 kilometers from Kismayo, a strategic port city 482 km south of Mogadishu.

Two teenage girls from Lake Manitoba First Nation, aged 14 and 13, face assault charges following a violent brawl after a minor-league hockey championship game. Their team, the Lake Manitoba First Nation Eagles, played against the Stonewall Blues. The Eagles received numerous penalties for rough play throughout the game. During the third period, one of the girls shot a puck at the officials during a stoppage in play, sparking a massive fight that involved players and spectators. Police were called to control the situation. The girl who shot the puck is charged with assault with a deadly weapon, and another teammate is charged with assault. Based on suspensions issued by Hockey Manitoba, the second charged player is likely one of two girls: the one suspended until January 1, 2015, for shooting the puck, or another suspended until December 15 for shoving an official. A third player is suspended until September 1, 2015, for punching a linesman and a referee. The RCMP has not confirmed which of these girls is the 13-year-old. The officials involved in the game have refused to officiate for Lake Manitoba next season. Some supporters of Lake Manitoba claim the referees used abusive language and were physically aggressive towards the players. A spectator, Margaret Swan, witnessed the alleged abuse and corroborated these claims.
Thirteen states have achieved record-low unemployment rates this year, marking the lowest levels since the federal government began tracking state-level data over four decades ago. Eight years after the Great Recession, states experiencing economic growth span the political spectrum, from states like Hawaii and California to states like Idaho and Texas. In October, Alabama, Hawaii, Maine, Mississippi, Tennessee, Texas, and Washington all reached their lowest unemployment rates ever recorded by the Bureau of Labor Statistics (BLS). California, Colorado, Idaho, North Dakota, and Oregon also hit new lows earlier this year. In contrast, during the depths of the Great Recession in 2010, six states—California, Colorado, Florida, Georgia, Nevada, and North Carolina—experienced record-high unemployment rates. Oregon, Rhode Island, and Kansas set their own record highs in 2009. Nationally, the unemployment rate stands at 4.1 percent, the lowest since December 2000 when it reached 3.9 percent. Thirteen states have unemployment rates significantly below the national average, while 15 states have rates significantly above it. Hawaii has the lowest unemployment rate in the nation at 2.2 percent in October, followed by North Dakota at 2.5 percent, and Colorado, Nebraska, and New Hampshire, each at 2.7 percent. Alaska has the highest unemployment rate at 7.2 percent, followed by the District of Columbia at 6.6 percent, and New Mexico at 6.1 percent. Alabama has seen the most significant decrease in unemployment over the past year, dropping 2.5 percentage points, according to the BLS. Tennessee's unemployment rate has also decreased by 2 percentage points, from 5 percent to 3 percent. Only Alaska, South Dakota, and the District of Columbia have experienced an increase in unemployment rates over the last 12 months.

A single father and his seven children, five girls and two boys, were found dead in their Maryland home on Monday due to carbon monoxide poisoning from a gas-powered generator. Police Chief Scott Keller stated that the children were in their beds and appeared to be asleep. Delmarva Power revealed that they had disconnected power to Rodney Todd's home after discovering an illegal connection to the grid. Todd, 36, had never legally connected to the grid during his residency there. Delmarva Power emphasized that the disconnection was for safety reasons and standard protocol, not due to unpaid bills. Following the power outage, Todd purchased a generator to heat his home, according to his stepfather, Lloyd Edwards. Russ Paulsen, executive director of community preparedness at the American Red Cross, explained that unpaid utility bills often lead people to use unsafe heating and lighting alternatives, sometimes with tragic consequences. He highlighted that residents in impoverished areas face six times the risk of house fires compared to those in wealthier areas, and a lack of smoke detectors exacerbates the danger. The Red Cross offers free smoke detectors and assistance to survivors of fires and carbon monoxide poisoning. Paulsen stressed that each smoke detector halves the risk of fire-related death. He warned that carbon monoxide impairs thinking, induces sleep, and can lead to death. Police are investigating the deaths and have requested Todd's home records from Delmarva Power. Friends and family described Todd as a dedicated father who was struggling to provide for his children. Tyisha Luneice Chambers, the children's mother, expressed her shock and regret, stating she would have helped if she had known about the power outage. Edwards described Todd as an outstanding father who bought a generator to keep his children warm, tragically leading to their deaths. Tom VanLandingham, director of Somerset County's Office of Home Energy Programs, confirmed that Todd had sought assistance with his heating bill in previous years but did not apply this year, leaving the reason a mystery. Bonnie Edwards, the children's grandmother, identified the boys as Cameron, 13, and ZhiHeem, 7, and the girls as Tyjuziana, 15, Tykeria, 12, Tynijuzia, 10, TyNiah, 9, and Tybreyia, 6.

In contrast, Washington Governor Jay Inslee issued a welcoming statement, drawing parallels to the internment of Japanese Americans during World War II and emphasizing the importance of not succumbing to fear. Washington has accepted a relatively small number of Syrian refugees since 2003, with only 38 settling in the state. However, Washington has welcomed 32,898 refugees overall since 2003, ranking seventh among states. California leads with 88,032 refugees, followed by Texas, New York, Florida, Minnesota, Arizona, and Washington. The majority of refugees in Washington come from the Middle East or predominantly Muslim countries, with Somalia and Iraq ranking as the third and fourth most common countries of origin. Seattle has received the highest number of refugees in the state, with nearly one-third of the total. Somalis constitute the largest refugee group in Seattle, numbering 2,560. Tukwila and Kent stand out for their high proportion of refugees relative to their population size, with close to 4% of each city's population being refugees.

Both Marshall Cogan, the former owner of ‘21’ from 1985 to 1995, and Ken Aretsky, the restaurant’s CEO from 1986 to 1995, confirmed that Trump's account was entirely wrong. A former economic advisor to Alex Salmond has warned that an independent Scotland keeping the pound in a formal currency union is impossible due to strict conditions set by London. Professor John Kay, a past member of the Council of Economic Advisers, told MSPs that negotiations for a currency deal would be extremely difficult for Scotland. Alex Salmond has yet to propose an alternative plan for Scotland's currency. This statement comes as a former chief economist for Scotland warned that no UK chancellor would risk taxpayers' money in such an arrangement. The SNP government desires to maintain the pound in a monetary union with the UK after independence, but this idea has been rejected by Chancellor George Osborne and his Labour counterpart, Ed Balls. Professor Kay explained to Holyrood’s finance committee that the common belief, particularly in the eurozone, is that a currency union necessitates fiscal backing. He added that while he personally disagrees with this notion, it holds significant weight in financial and political circles. He believes that if Scotland voted for independence and pursued a monetary union, the UK Treasury would impose demanding conditions in negotiations, which would be challenging for a Scottish government to accept. These conditions could include UK control over Scotland's banking system and fiscal policy, which the UK is unlikely to relinquish. This, according to Professor Kay, is the fundamental obstacle that would likely lead to the failure of these negotiations. If Scotland agreed to these UK demands, it would relinquish most of the economic policy autonomy it aimed to gain through independence. The committee was gathering evidence on Scotland's public finances following the referendum. Gavin McCrone, a former chief economist at the Scottish Office, echoed this sentiment, stating that he doubts any UK chancellor would risk the finances of the rest of the country for Scottish debt. He believes this is the primary reason George Osborne and others oppose currency union. However, he acknowledges that negotiations would likely still take place.

The Supreme Court has directed the Drugs Technical Advisory Board (DTAB) to review the safety, efficacy, and therapeutic justification of fixed dose combination (FDC) drugs. The DTAB, or a sub-committee appointed by it, must consult all stakeholders and submit its report to the government within six months. The court's order excludes 15 FDCs licensed before 1988 and maintains the status quo for the 329 FDCs under review. The All India Drugs Action Network welcomed the decision, stating that it allows the government to eliminate irrational, unscientific, and hazardous FDCs. They noted that the 344 FDCs in question represent only 5% of the total FDC market value in India, with approximately half considered irrational. Pfizer Ltd, whose Corex cough syrup was among the banned FDCs, expressed support for the government's goal of eliminating unapproved and irrational drug combinations. The center had appealed against a 2016 Delhi High Court ruling that overturned its 2016 ban on 344 FDC drugs. The Supreme Court had previously stayed proceedings in all high courts regarding the ban. FDC drugs contain two or more active ingredients in a fixed dosage ratio. The ban affected approximately 6,000 brands from major pharmaceutical companies. The ban was implemented following a report by a committee headed by Chandrakant Kokate, which identified 963 FDCs as "irrational" and posing health risks. The Delhi High Court had quashed the notification, stating that the government failed to consult statutory authorities like the DTAB and the Drugs Consultative Committee before implementing the ban.
Britain's economy is expected to slow in the second half of 2017, according to a significant survey. This news may disappoint some Bank of England officials who are hoping to raise interest rates for the first time in ten years. The Markit/CIPS survey revealed that growth in British service companies decreased to a four-month low in June. Although the survey indicated the economy recovered some speed in the second quarter, potentially expanding at a rate of around 0.4 percent, double the pace of the first quarter, there are concerning signs in the survey's forward-looking indicators. Business expectations reached their lowest point since last July's dip following the Brexit vote and were close to lows seen in late 2011. New order growth fell to a nine-month low. The headline index dropped to a four-month low of 53.4 in June, down from 53.8 in May, just below the forecast of 53.5 in a Reuters poll of economists. On Tuesday, Bank of England rate-setter Michael Saunders expressed confidence that lower consumer spending would be offset by increased exports and investment, justifying his vote to raise interest rates from a record low of 0.25 percent. However, JPMorgan economist Allan Monks believes the latest PMI survey contradicts this assumption. He notes that the weakness in the UK PMI occurs while broader European PMIs have strengthened significantly. IHS Markit suggests that uncertainty following June's election, where Prime Minister Theresa May lost her parliamentary majority, Brexit negotiations, and the overall economic outlook likely impacted the mood among service firms. A third consecutive monthly decline in car sales in June, although still from recent record highs, highlights the slowdown in consumer spending. Separate official data revealed that productivity, a significant economic concern for Britain over the past decade, decreased in the first three months of the year, the first decline since late 2015. This latest productivity data further complicates the outlook for Bank of England policymakers. While weak productivity limits future wage growth, it also coincides with higher inflation. Ian Brinkley, acting chief economist at the Chartered Institute of Personnel and Development, states that unless productivity improves, people's wages and living standards will continue to decline, and the UK will be ill-equipped to compete after leaving the EU.

A declassified State Department report from May 1964 indicated that India had the potential to develop nuclear weapons as early as that year. The report, released by the National Security Archive and the Nuclear Proliferation International History Project, highlighted frequent changes in the fuel core of the Canadian-Indian Reactor (CIR) at Trombay, occurring as often as every six months. US intelligence at the time expressed concerns about India's nuclear intentions. The State Department Bureau of Intelligence and Research (INR) stated in the report that India was capable of initiating nuclear weapons development if it chose to do so. While no evidence of a weapons program existed, the INR noted that a six-month timeframe for fuel core changes was too short for typical research reactor operations, suggesting a potential use for producing weapons-grade plutonium. Despite the lack of evidence for a weapons program, the INR deemed it unlikely that India had decided against building a nuclear bomb. However, the report emphasized that India's actions to date were conducive to future weapons development. The State Department report suggested that India's leadership might have had nationalistic motivations for building the Phoenix plant, but if it desired a nuclear weapons capability, it would pursue it. The report concluded that India had made the first deliberate step towards acquiring a nuclear weapon, meaning they had access to or the ability to produce weapons-grade plutonium. By 2013, India's estimated nuclear arsenal reached at least 110 weapons. Although India has signed and ratified the Biological Weapons Convention and the Chemical Weapons Convention, it has not joined the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty, citing its allowance of nuclear arsenals for the US, Russia, China, the UK, and France without disarmament obligations.
At least 42 people, primarily Christians celebrating Christmas, have died and dozens have become ill after consuming poisonous alcohol in Pakistan, according to the Dawn newspaper. Police in Punjab province detained all alcohol sellers in Toba Tek Singh, the location of the incident, for questioning, a local police official, Muhammad Shahbaz, informed DPA. Shahbaz stated that the alcoholic beverage was consumed during Christmas festivities in a Christian community. Five Muslims were also among the deceased. Many victims had been drinking and dancing throughout Sunday and into Monday morning when some fell ill and were hospitalized. Approximately 150 others were taken to clinics, many unconscious. Funerals have been held for 25 victims, Shahbaz said. The individual suspected of arranging the alcohol for the Christmas celebrations was among the dead, making it more difficult for police to determine the origin of the toxic beverage. Deaths from contaminated alcohol, often homemade, occur periodically in Pakistan, a Muslim-majority country, frequently around religious holidays. In March, at least 40 people, mostly Hindus, died after consuming poisonous alcohol days before Holi. The sale and consumption of alcohol is illegal for Muslims in Pakistan, and minorities require permits to purchase it in limited quantities. Christians constitute about 1.6 percent of Pakistan's 190 million people, with most residing in Punjab.

A new 57km motorway connecting Gort and Tuam in County Galway has opened. The M17/M18 project, costing almost half a billion euros, is anticipated to significantly reduce travel times to and from the northwest by bypassing several traffic congestion points. Construction of the toll-free motorway took several years following almost fifteen years of planning. The new motorway will alleviate traffic congestion in towns and villages like Tuam, Claregalway, and Clarinbridge for motorists traveling to and from the northwest. However, there are concerns that traffic issues from those areas may shift to Galway city, where congestion on the eastern approaches is frequent. Transport Infrastructure Ireland and Galway County Council view the motorway opening as part of a larger strategy to manage increased traffic volumes. The next phase involves constructing a bypass for Galway city, but this proposal is already facing controversy in the affected areas and is expected to be challenged during the planning process in the coming months.

Although California is experiencing a drought despite the El Nino winter, Marin County is largely unaffected. While water restrictions remain in place, six out of seven reservoirs are at capacity, with the remaining one at 95 percent. The Marin Municipal Water District attributes the county's success to residents' continued water conservation efforts. Crystal Yezman, who manages the Tamalpais watershed for the district, reports that the seven reservoirs are currently at 98 percent capacity. This is in stark contrast to the rest of California, where drought conditions persist. Yezman notes that Marin County's near-full reservoirs are unusual. The county's water abundance has even led to a resurgence in the Coho salmon population. Gregory Andrew, the fishery program manager, observed and counted one of 500 Coho salmon that returned to Lagunitas Creek this year, marking the best showing in a decade. This success is attributed to well-spaced, early storms that allowed the salmon to swim upstream in December. Andrew acknowledges that while the Coho population on Lagunitas Creek is having a good year, it remains tenuous.
Rhus javanica, a medicinal herb, has demonstrated oral antiviral activity against herpes simplex virus (HSV) in mice. Researchers purified two primary anti-HSV compounds, moronic acid and betulonic acid, from the herbal extract using ethyl acetate extraction at pH 10 followed by chromatographic separations. They then assessed their anti-HSV activity both in vitro and in vivo. Moronic acid was the most abundant anti-HSV compound in the ethyl acetate-soluble fraction. In vitro, moronic acid and betulonic acid exhibited effective concentrations for 50% plaque reduction against wild-type HSV type 1 (HSV-1) of 3.9 and 2.6 microgram/ml, respectively. Moronic acid had a larger therapeutic index (10.3-16.3) compared to betulonic acid (6.2). Moronic acid demonstrated similar efficacy against acyclovir-phosphonoacetic acid-resistant HSV-1, thymidine kinase-deficient HSV-1, and wild-type HSV type 2. Oral administration of moronic acid to mice infected with HSV-1 three times daily significantly delayed the development of skin lesions and prolonged the survival of infected mice without causing toxicity. Moronic acid suppressed viral loads in the brain more effectively than in the skin, correlating with the observed prolongation of survival. These findings indicate that moronic acid, a major anti-HSV compound extracted from Rhus javanica, exhibits a distinct mechanism of action compared to acyclovir (ACV) and demonstrates novel oral therapeutic efficacy against HSV-infected mice.
A new law signed by the governor on Monday will allow judges to revoke a driver's license for up to 10 years if they have three or more DUI convictions within a decade. Currently, the maximum license revocation for repeat offenders is three years. The law, proposed by Assemblyman Jerry Hill, D-San Mateo, is expected to remove over 10,000 repeat DUI offenders from California roads annually. Hill cited statistics showing nearly 188,000 DUI convictions in 2008, with 9,164 being third offenses and 3,200 having four or more. Hill urged judges to utilize this new authority to enhance road safety. According to the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration, there are 1.5 million DUI arrests in California each year, with one-third being repeat offenders. Over 310,000 Californians have three or more DUI convictions. Governor Arnold Schwarzenegger, a Republican, stated that the legislation is a crucial step in making California's roads safer and that those with multiple DUI convictions should not be driving.
Abnormal animal behavior can be defined in several ways. Statistically, it refers to behaviors with a significantly different occurrence, frequency, or intensity compared to the average. This means almost any behavior could be considered abnormal in an individual. Less formally, "abnormal" encompasses any activity judged outside the typical behavior pattern for that species or age group. For example, infanticide might be normal in one species but abnormal in another if it becomes too frequent. This definition excludes one-time behaviors in individual animals unless they are repeated by others and recognized as part of the species' typical behavior. Most abnormal behaviors can be grouped together, such as elimination, feeding, or stereotypies, but many fall into multiple categories. Some abnormal behaviors might be caused by environmental factors like captivity, while others stem from medical conditions. This list does not include behaviors in genetically modified animals exhibiting abnormal traits.

Retail stores are carefully designed with spatial arrangements intended to influence shopper behavior. For example, essential items are placed in the back, forcing customers to pass by higher-margin products first. Stores are often laid out in a clockwise pattern to accommodate right-handed shoppers. Pleasant scents are also used to encourage impulsivity. However, these tactics are becoming less effective as consumers enter stores with a clear shopping list, influenced by online shopping habits. Retailers are noticing a shift in consumer behavior, as people are buying only what they need and resisting impulse purchases. To counteract these strategies, some shoppers employ their own methods. For example, some couples shop together to keep each other accountable, while others avoid using shopping carts to prevent overbuying.
A fire in Manila's Tondo slum district left over 15,000 people homeless and injured seven residents. The blaze, which began Tuesday evening in a shanty house, destroyed 1,200 homes before being extinguished Wednesday morning after a 10-hour battle. Regional fire chief Senior Superintendent Wilfredo Tiu reported that the injured residents were trying to save their belongings before evacuating. Three evacuation centers were established, providing food and water to the 3,000 families displaced by the fire. However, hours after the fire was contained, many residents remained on a nearby road with their possessions, including appliances. The fire also disrupted traffic, blocking delivery trucks to and from the port. While investigators are still determining the cause, past incidents in the area have been attributed to faulty electrical wiring due to the prevalence of illegal connections.

MongoDB v3.4 offers a $graphLookup aggregation pipeline operator for recursive searches within a collection. This operator can be used to traverse document hierarchies. For instance, using the provided document structure, the following aggregation query could be executed: db.collectionName.aggregate([ {$unwind:{ path:"$childrenIdList", preserveNullAndEmptyArrays: true} }, {$graphLookup:{ from:"collectionName", startWith:"$_id", connectFromField:"_id", connectToField:"childrenIdList", as:"myparents", restrictSearchWithMatch: {"_id"}} }, {$match: {"_id": 7 } }, {$group:{ _id:"$_id", parents:{$addToSet:"$myparents._id"} }} ]) This query would return a result similar to: { "_id" : 7, "parents" : [ [ 1, 2, 4 ] ] } However, for large collections, this query might not be efficient due to the $unwind operation performed on each document, preventing index utilization. Therefore, it's recommended to reconsider the document model structure for optimal performance. Data Models Tree Structures provides further guidance. The document schema should be flexible and optimized based on application logic and querying needs.

Last week, with the Freedom of Press Foundation's support, I visited Guantanamo during a mass hunger strike. I toured the detention facility with four other journalists, seeing camps 5 and 6, Camp X-Ray, the hospital, library, and food preparation areas. We also observed the prisoners' morning prayer. The military-led tour was structured but insightful. They emphasized the prisoners' ingratitude and the guards' patience despite being subjected to bodily fluids. Medical staff stated that no hunger-striking prisoner who received tube feeding had complained about the process, a finding I detailed in an Al Jazeera report. We left the island on Friday, the 100th day of the strike. During our visit, the number of fasting prisoners increased by three, reaching 103. Thirty are being force-fed, according to the government. Navy Capt. Robert Durand, a Guantanamo spokesman, said the military won't negotiate with the prisoners to end the hunger strike. Some photographs were reviewed and cropped by the military to conceal surveillance cameras, guard towers, and other sensitive landmarks. The remaining photographs can be viewed on Flickr.

George Clooney's Satellite Sentinel Project, launched to monitor the escalating violence in Sudan, released a report on June 17, 2011, with compelling evidence of a systematic campaign of civilian killings in Kadugli, South Kordofan. The project, first covered by TIME, utilized eyewitness accounts and DigitalGlobe satellite imagery to identify what appear to be mass graves and bodies in Kadugli. The report details four eyewitness accounts of Sudan Armed Forces and allied militias searching homes in Kadugli and systematically killing civilians suspected of supporting southern Sudanese forces. Accounts describe the slitting of one civilian's throat and the sealing and burning of homes with civilians inside. Eyewitnesses also reported mass graves, visible in satellite imagery, dug near Tilo School in Kadugli on June 8. The pits, approximately 26 meters long and 5 meters wide, were reportedly dug by a yellow earth mover. Witnesses observed large green trucks transporting approximately 100 bodies to the site for burial. Before burial, some bodies were reportedly wrapped in white plastic and piled in Kadugli. Satellite imagery captured what appears to be this pile of bodies.

Facebook announced today that its app will soon be available on Apple TV for the first time. The app, as previously rumored, will prioritize video playback with personalized features. Facebook first revealed the development of its video app earlier this month and confirmed the news today in a blog post detailing upcoming video features. The new app allows users to watch videos from friends, Pages they follow, popular live videos, and recommended videos based on their interests. Users can also access saved videos, previously watched content, shared videos, and uploaded videos. Previously, Facebook recommended using AirPlay from the iOS app to watch videos on Apple TV. The dedicated Facebook app for Apple TV will provide a standalone method for watching videos on a larger screen. Facebook stated that its new video player will be launched soon on Apple TV, as well as on other platforms like Amazon Fire TV and Samsung Smart TV. Alongside the new app, Facebook announced the rollout of auto-play sound for videos, coming soon to all users. While the feature might seem disruptive, Facebook outlined how to disable it: if a phone is set to silent, videos will not play with sound, and users can turn off "Videos in News Feed Start With Sound" in Settings to prevent videos from playing with sound. Facebook also plans to display in-app messages to inform users about the new sound experience and control options. Other video-related updates include improved vertical video playback and picture-in-picture (PIP) playback within the Facebook app. Facebook mentioned that Android users will be able to utilize PIP even when the Facebook app is closed, but the announcement did not specify iPad support, which also supports PIP on iOS.

Thai police arrested three local officials and a Myanmar national for allegedly trafficking and holding Rohingya Muslims for ransom at a jungle camp in southern Thailand where 26 bodies were found in shallow graves last week. National police chief Gen. Somyot Poompanmoung stated the suspects, including two deputy village chiefs and a municipal councillor, were part of a transnational crime network involving individuals from Thailand, Myanmar, and Malaysia that had been operating for three to four years. The discovery of the clandestine camp, a known trafficking hub for migrants seeking better lives abroad, highlighted the persistence of the problem despite authorities' assurances of addressing its root causes. This incident further damaged Thailand's image, following recent revelations about human trafficking allegedly fueled by corrupt Thai officials. Last June, the United States placed Thailand in its lowest category, Tier 3, for its efforts in combating human trafficking. Thailand has pledged action to improve its standing, but its reputation suffered further after reports by the Associated Press revealed that some Thai fishing vessels employed men from Myanmar, Cambodia, and Laos as forced labor or slaves. Somyot said police are investigating reports of more trafficking camps hidden in the mountainous jungles of southern Thailand. They are searching Khao Kaew mountain in Songkla province, near the Malaysian border, and plan to expand their search to other dense jungle areas. “We will not stop here. We will search the widest area until we are certain there are no other camps left,” Somyot told reporters. “We will find out if there are more.” A lone survivor from the camp, hospitalized due to severe malnutrition, informed authorities that smugglers escaped earlier last week with around 100 Rohingya. Members of this religious minority have faced state-sanctioned discrimination in predominantly Buddhist Myanmar for decades. Mob attacks in recent years have killed up to 280 people, triggering one of the largest exoduses since the Vietnam War.

Inspired by recent discussions about migration and its impact on society and economic growth, we present four charts exploring labor mobility in the EU. The first chart displays the net migration rate (immigration minus emigration) per 1000 inhabitants in Europe in 2012. High net immigration is represented in green, while high net emigration is represented in red. This heat map reveals significant variation across EU countries. For example, Germany and Belgium have net inflows of 4.3 and 6.5 migrants per 1000 inhabitants, respectively, while Spain and Greece have net outflows of -3 and -4, indicating more people leaving than arriving. The following two charts examine the relationship between unemployment rates and net migration rates. The first chart displays the levels, while the second shows changes between 2007 and 2012. Countries hit hardest by the economic crisis (experiencing larger increases in unemployment) have seen a greater outflow of people and lower net migration. Generally, countries with weaker economic performance have experienced larger net outflows, with those leaving primarily moving to countries with stronger economies. The final chart presents the population living in core European nations by country of citizenship in 2013. The member states on the right and left represent the main sending countries, while those in the center are the main host countries in core Europe. Economic migration not only acts as a macroeconomic stabilizer, which is crucial for an optimal currency area, but also provides microeconomic benefits. Labor mobility enhances the potential for matching firms with suitable workers, addresses skills shortages, and promotes international commerce by reducing cultural barriers to trade.

Emacs can be used to interact with the MSVS C++ compiler through the command line. VS provides batch files for setting the necessary environment variables. A .bat file can be created to take arguments, execute the VS .bat file for setting environment variables, and then execute those arguments as a command. From Emacs, you can use M-x compile vc2013.bat <command>. Yes, there are C++ teams that use both Emacs and Visual Studio. For example, in a Scala project, some programmers might use Eclipse, while others use Emacs with ENSIME, and they can work together effectively. Similarly, in a C++ project on Windows, Emacs and Visual Studio can coexist. The VS IDE can be used for debugging, while the majority of coding is done in Emacs. CMake can be used to create VC project files, makefiles, etc., from a single build specification.
BACtrack, a company that makes portable breathalyzers that connect to smartphones, analyzed nearly 300,000 breathalyzer tests taken nationwide in 2014. As expected, the drunkest days were St. Patrick's Day and New Year's Eve. Surprisingly, Waltham, Massachusetts had the highest average BAC in December. BACtrack's report shows that 14 of the 15 biggest drinking days are in the winter, with the exception of May 3rd, which fell before Cinco de Mayo. Besides New Year's Eve and Super Bowl Sunday, the day after Valentine's Day had a high average BAC of 0.092, perhaps because of lovelorn singles. Waltham had the highest recorded BAC in December at 0.133%, slightly higher than Jersey City, New Jersey (0.132%), Champlin, Minnesota (0.124%), and New Orleans, Louisiana (0.123%). However, it's unlikely Waltham is the drunkest city in America. While 300,000 breathalyzer tests seem like a large number, they represent a small sample size. Massachusetts likely had around 12,000 tests, with Waltham contributing about 10. Even if Waltham residents were more likely to use BACtrack due to its proximity to Boston, 20 tests in December is still a tiny sample. Someone with a BAC of 0.133% would likely use the breathalyzer multiple times throughout the night. Therefore, it's more likely that a few people in Waltham used the breathalyzer frequently on a single night, leading to the high average BAC. This highlights the importance of understanding the context behind data. Regardless, please use a designated driver during the holidays.

California is nearing the possibility of having the third presidential primary in the country. The state Senate overwhelmingly approved a bill to move future primary elections to March. Senate Bill 568 is one of two bills being considered this year that would change California's primary from its current June date, which is among the latest in the nation. The bill passed with a bipartisan vote of 32-6. State Senator Ricardo Lara (D-Bell Gardens), the author of the bill, believes California needs a stronger voice in national decisions. He stated in a written statement, "California leads the nation in clean energy, farming, job growth, and the diversity of our people. The Prime Time Primary Bill would make us one of the first states to hold a presidential primary, ensuring our state's voters have a voice in the national debate."
An Arkansas judge has ruled that over 500 same-sex marriages performed in the state last year must be recognized. This decision allows the couples to access benefits like filing joint tax returns. Pulaski County Circuit Judge Wendell Griffen made the ruling on Tuesday, June 9th, following the invalidation of Arkansas's gay marriage ban by another judge. The Arkansas Supreme Court had temporarily halted the issuance of marriage licenses to same-sex couples in May 2014 after a voter-approved ban was put in place. Griffen's ruling grants the couples the right to file joint tax returns, enroll in state health insurance plans together, appear on a child's birth certificate, and even file for divorce. However, the state has the option to appeal the ruling.

The delegation consists of Lahore Commissioner Rashid Mehmood Langrial, Lahore Development Authority (LDA) Director-General Ahad Khan Cheema, LDA's strategic policy unit chief Moazzam Sipra, and Mustafa Kamal Chaudhry, a technical expert on urban infrastructure projects. The delegation aims to study the SRDP and replicate its model in Lahore, as the LDA is working on the feasibility of the Ravi Riverfront Urban Development Project (RRUDP) with the assistance of a foreign consultancy firm. Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif expressed interest in the SRDP after learning about it and urged officials to visit it promptly. The visit, initially planned for May, was postponed due to officials' prior commitments. Sharif, during meetings on the RRUDP at his residence, repeatedly instructed officials to visit India and learn from the SRDP in Ahmedabad. The delegation, led by the commissioner, will enter India through the Wagah Border and travel to Amritsar to catch a flight to New Delhi. The SRDP is a major development initiative accomplished by Indian Prime Minister Narendra Modi during his tenure as Chief Minister of Gujarat.
Louisiana lawmakers will convene later this month to examine the practicality and effectiveness of marijuana legalization in the state. The House Criminal Justice Committee, at the request of State Rep. Dalton Honore, will hold a meeting on January 21st to explore the potential for legalization. Despite this, there is currently no legislation proposing marijuana legalization in Louisiana. Louisiana currently enforces some of the most severe marijuana penalties in the nation. Possession of any amount of marijuana, even a single joint, can result in up to six months in jail for a first offense. A second offense carries a five-year prison sentence and a $2,500 fine, while a third offense leads to a felony conviction with a mandatory minimum 20-year prison sentence. Last year, an attempt to mitigate these harsh penalties—reducing the second offense to a maximum sentence of two years and subsequent offenses to a maximum of eight, while eliminating the mandatory minimum sentence—was passed by the House but lacked sufficient Senate support for debate and a vote. The Legislative Fiscal Office estimated that this change would have saved the state $2.2 million in 2014 through reduced incarceration costs had it been successful. Rep. Austin Badon, who introduced last year's bill, has reintroduced a similar bill for 2014, House Bill 14. If enacted, this bill would cap jail time for a second marijuana arrest at two years and a $500 fine, and would cap third and subsequent offenses at a maximum of five years in jail and a maximum $2,000 fine. Recent polls indicate that fifty-three percent of Louisiana voters support legalizing marijuana for adults aged 21 and older and regulating its sale.

According to the New York Times, Facebook has hired experts who previously worked on the iPhone and other smartphones. The Environmental Molecular Sciences Laboratory (EMSL), a national scientific user facility sponsored by the Department of Energy, focuses on finding molecular-level solutions for energy and environmental challenges. Located at the Pacific Northwest National Laboratory, EMSL supports both public and private research, collaborating with nearly 800 scientists annually from various sectors and countries. Researchers can utilize EMSL's in-house tools or build their own specialized instruments. EMSL's high-performance supercomputer, Cascade, is used for diverse studies, including aerosol impacts and water purification. Recently, researchers used EMSL's capabilities, including the Chinook supercomputer, to investigate the diverse cloud types over oceans, crucial for climate projections. Current climate models often average various oceanic cloud types into a single category, such as stratocumulus or cumulus clouds, which can lead to inaccuracies. Researchers confirmed that these averaged models underestimate actual ocean cloud cover, resulting in an overestimation of solar energy reaching the ocean surface. They also found that these models underestimate temperature variations and cloud layer movement, leading to inaccurate predictions of cloud type transitions and uncertainties in climate projections. These findings at EMSL have the potential to improve the accuracy of climate projections.
A new zero-day exploit for Internet Explorer versions 7, 8, and 9 on Windows XP, Vista, and 7 is available in Metasploit. Visiting a malicious website can compromise a computer, granting the attacker the same privileges as the current user. As Microsoft has not yet released a patch for this vulnerability, users are strongly advised to switch to browsers like Chrome or Firefox until a security update is available. This exploit has already been used by attackers in the wild. It affects approximately 41% of Internet users in North America and 32% globally (source: StatCounter). The Metasploit team added the zero-day exploit module to allow the security community to test their systems' vulnerability and develop countermeasures. This exploit follows another recently disclosed Java exploit released by the Metasploit team. Researchers Eric Romang and @binjo discovered this new exploit, which targets a Microsoft Internet Explorer use-after-free vulnerability. The Metasploit team collaborated with Romang and @binjo to create a functional exploit. Users can download Metasploit to access the exploit. Screenshots demonstrate successful attacks against Windows 7 with Internet Explorer 9, Windows 7 with Internet Explorer 8, and a fully patched Windows XP SP3 system. The exploit also works against Windows Vista. For more information and updates, refer to the Metasploit blog.
The controversial GP fee could be implemented without parliamentary approval, similar to the government's recent bypassing of parliament to introduce petrol indexation. Health Minister Peter Dutton has refused to rule out using a regulation to reduce Medicare rebates by $5, aiming for the $3.5 billion in savings outlined in the budget. The government faces difficulty securing Senate approval for legislation to impose the fee, which is opposed by the Australian Medical Association and other key health groups. The Opposition, the Greens, and the Palmer United Party have stated they will not support the fee. The AMA expresses concern that the government might bypass parliament to implement the fee, as they did with the petrol excise indexation measure. AMA president Dr. Brian Owler suggests this could result in a cut to rebates, reducing funds for patients and GPs, ultimately harming healthcare. The Medicare rebate is determined by a Medicare schedule fee set by regulation. The government could introduce a regulation to reduce the current $37.05 schedule fee for a GP visit by $5, achieving the desired budget savings. This would effectively cut the Medicare rebate by $5. When asked about this possibility, Health Minister Peter Dutton declined to comment. AMA GP spokesman Dr. Brian Morton states that if the government proceeds with this plan, many GPs would become unviable unless they see more patients or charge an additional $5. He argues that this would ultimately harm prevention and reduce hospital admissions. While parliament has the power to disallow any regulation Mr. Dutton introduces, if implemented in the final sitting in June next year, the measure would take effect on July 1 and could not be disallowed until August 18. There is a possibility that the Medicare rebate could be eliminated entirely if the Opposition and crossbenchers in the Senate attempt to block the fee cut. By forcing the Senate to disallow a $5 rebate cut, the government gains a one-vote advantage, as it only needs 38 votes to block a disallowance motion, compared to 39 votes needed to pass legislation.
Patrick Wildt reports that the g2k16 hackathon was highly productive. They successfully switched armv7 from OABI to EABI, a standard expected by all ports. This change eliminated support for Zaurus, allowing the team to focus on ARMv7 and leverage its features, such as the hardware floating point unit. Improvements to C++ exceptions are also expected. The team also integrated LLVM and the new C++ stack into the project, with libc++, libc++abi, and libunwind imported by coordinated efforts. While the hackathon concluded with these significant achievements, the team acknowledges the substantial work still ahead.

YouTube is finally allowing users to choose the Material Design version on desktop without needing a URL workaround, after over a year of testing. The redesign aims to make the desktop experience more similar to the mobile version, with a simpler look that emphasizes content. The new design includes infinite scrolling, a continuous user profile banner, and a larger hero video on user profile pages. In theater mode, the top search bar turns black to match the rest of the screen. Dark Mode can be enabled natively by clicking under the user icon and changing settings. The update features more white space, a lighter color search bar, and aligns the YouTube username under videos with current views. While the preview is available globally to YouTubers, it will only be accessible to a limited number of users initially. Once a certain percentage of users opt in, the preview may be closed to better manage user feedback. YouTube plans to gradually roll out the redesign to more users. Around 1 percent of users will receive the update automatically, with plans to increase this percentage every few weeks. During last year's trial, roughly 1 to 3 percent of users had access to Material Design. The team aims to fully implement the change within a few months, depending on user demand and feedback. To access the Material Design preview, visit YouTube.com/new. Users who prefer the classic version can revert by selecting "Restore classic YouTube" under their account menu.

The feature is currently in a testing phase and is expected to be released within two weeks. Users can send an SMS message as soon as they start typing a phone number into Chat's search box. U.S. healthcare is undeniably expensive, but how bad is it really? National Geographic's graphic provides a clear illustration of the three main issues: cost, outcomes, and access. The graphic displays several countries along an axis representing annual per capita healthcare spending on the left and average life expectancy on the right. The lines connecting these axes depict a country's healthcare costs and its citizens' health. Ideally, a line should slope upwards, indicating a positive correlation between spending and health outcomes. A downward slope suggests inefficient spending. The U.S. doesn't even appear on the chart, highlighting its exceptionally high costs. Furthermore, the graphic addresses healthcare access by using line thickness to represent the frequency of doctor visits. Countries like Japan and the Czech Republic demonstrate high access, while the U.S. falls short.

While it might seem sufficient to have connections elsewhere, like online, research suggests that feeling connected within your community is important. Kim and his team controlled for individual social connectedness in their study, also accounting for factors like optimism, age, race, income, marital status, education, mental health, and heart attack risk factors such as diabetes, obesity, and high blood pressure. Although the study only shows a correlation, Kim believes that increasing community involvement likely won't be harmful. He suggests that community connection might lead to people looking out for each other's health, sharing information, offering support, and creating a sense of safety. Kim, a positive psychologist, emphasizes the importance of emotional support in managing stress. Positive psychology, pioneered by researchers like Martin Seligman, focuses on the benefits of optimism and purpose in life. Kim's work has shown that optimism is linked to a lower risk of heart failure and stroke.

Before environmental regulations existed, logging stripped forests bare, hunting decimated wildlife, and companies polluted water sources with toxic waste. The Cuyahoga River in Cleveland, Ohio, frequently caught fire before the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) was formed. Polluted with oil, sewage, garbage, and industrial waste, the river killed fish and sometimes ignited. The worst fire occurred in 1952, but even after an oil slick ignited that year, people continued to dump waste into the river. A final fire in 1969 led to the Clean Water Act and the EPA's creation. The Clean Water Act aims to protect American drinking water sources from pollution. It requires companies to implement newer technologies that reduce waste. Smog, a smoky fog found in many cities, is visible air pollution. Car exhaust, coal burning, and other harmful substances contribute to smog, which can damage lungs and trigger allergy-like reactions. Recent U.S. regulations on factory emissions aim to reduce air pollution and mitigate smog. The EPA's Superfund program, established in 1980, cleans up hazardous waste sites. Industrial companies dumped toxic substances like lead and arsenic, contaminating the air and groundwater, posing risks to people and the environment. The Berkeley Pit in Montana is a Superfund site where mining waste contaminated soil, groundwater, and surface water with heavy metals like aluminum, arsenic, mercury, and lead. The EPA has been working on the site since the late 1980s, but it remains a health risk. The U.S. lacked logging regulations in the past, resulting in the clearing of vast forests. Laws like the National Forest Management Act of 1976 help protect forests from excessive timber harvesting. The American buffalo, nearly hunted to extinction, serves as an example of the need for environmental regulations. Today, they survive in small numbers thanks to government intervention. The Endangered Species Act protects endangered plants and animals.

Cheaper, smaller, and lighter batteries may soon be a reality thanks to a discovery by researchers at the University of Texas at Austin. They have developed a new family of anode materials called the Interdigitated Eutectic Alloy (IdEA) anode, which can double the charge capacity of lithium-ion batteries. This breakthrough could lead to more efficient batteries for a wide range of applications, from cellphones to large-scale energy storage systems. The IdEA anode is produced in just two simple steps, significantly reducing time and material costs compared to the traditional multi-step process. This new material is also incredibly thin and lightweight, being one-quarter the thickness and half the weight of the graphite and copper anodes currently used in most lithium-ion batteries. This means smaller, lighter rechargeable batteries could be possible in the future. Professor Arumugam Manthiram, who led the research team, expressed excitement about the development of a cost-effective and scalable process for producing electrode nanomaterials. He believes the material has the potential to make a commercially viable advancement in lithium-ion battery technology. Unlike recent efforts that have focused on building nanomaterials atom by atom, Manthiram and his team, including postdoctoral fellow Karl Kreder and graduate student Brian Heligman, created a new class of anode materials by mechanically rolling eutectic metal alloys into nanostructured metal foils. Currently, graphite powder coated on a copper foil serves as the primary anode for mass-produced lithium-ion batteries. However, the copper adds unnecessary bulk without improving battery performance, and the manufacturing process is complex and time-consuming. The IdEA anode eliminates this complicated coating process, drastically simplifying production. Kreder, the lead author of the study, realized that a micrometer-scale alloy anode could be transformed into a nanomaterial using traditional metallurgical alloying processes. He explained that the eutectic microstructure forms naturally due to thermodynamics, and rolling the microstructure reduces it to a nanostructure, a remarkably inexpensive process. The resulting anodes occupy significantly less space, overcoming a key barrier to commercializing better batteries for portable electronic devices like cellphones and medical devices, as well as larger applications like electric vehicles. This research was published in the journal ACS Energy Letters and was funded by a grant from the U.S. Department of Energy’s Office of Basic Energy Sciences, Division of Materials Sciences and Engineering.
While you can't always control lighting or background in pictures, you can influence other elements. Blue is generally considered a flattering color for photos and television, so wearing it for special occasions can enhance your appearance. Similar colors like green and purple may have the same effect. Colors like yellow or red can be trickier to wear well in photos, but it depends on your skin tone. Wearing colors too close to your skin tone can be unflattering. The angle of your face also matters. Whether you turn your head slightly, tilt your chin, or look directly at the camera can impact how you look. The photographer is best equipped to advise on the most flattering angle for each photo, assuming they have a good sense of aesthetics. For casual photos like those for Facebook, don't stress too much about posing. Study your existing photos to see which angles and expressions look best on you and apply that knowledge to future pictures. Hopefully, these tips have helped you learn how to look more photogenic.
