There's a prevalent obsession, particularly in mainstream media, with whether hybrid, plug-in hybrid, or electric vehicles will "pay for themselves" through fuel savings. A recent example is the misleading article "The payoff for plug-in hybrids: 95 years?". While scrutinizing claims that a specific car will save money is reasonable, the focus has shifted, with many believing that financial savings are the sole purpose of eco-friendly transportation and the primary metric for evaluating new green cars. This is absurd. Cars are rarely expected to "pay for themselves." No one questions someone who chooses a larger V6 engine, sunroof, or nicer wheels based on aesthetics or performance, even if these upgrades don't offer financial returns. Why then, are those who opt for cleaner cars due to environmental concerns, enjoyment of electric driving, or support for new technology subjected to financial justification? Investing in a more efficient vehicle, at least partially, offsets the cost through savings. Conversely, a large engine incurs higher initial and operational expenses. Let's eliminate the double standard regarding green car finances unless the genuine question is "will it pay for itself?"
Jakob de Haan, head of research at De Nederlandsche Bank (DNB), recently addressed questions about bitcoin's role in global finance at the reinvent.money conference. During the September 26th speech, de Haan was asked about bitcoin, including questions that criticized the euro. A video shows de Haan responding to a question about whether DNB fears being replaced by decentralized financial technologies like bitcoin, suggesting the institution remains open-minded about the technology. He stated, "Apparently, there are a few people convinced [digital currencies] are the future. Let’s wait and see. I’m not opposed to all these initiatives and if you’re right, the world will be very different and I will lose my job, but by then I’ll be retired I guess." De Haan acknowledged he's not a bitcoin expert but that DNB is closely monitoring the technology and researchers are studying it. He emphasized, "You might think we are fundamentally opposed to these alternatives, we are not." DNB has taken steps to inform the public that digital currencies, while accepted at some locations, lack the same consumer protections as traditional options, as stated in May 2014. De Haan called this guidance "important" and countered a question about the benefits of private currencies competing with public ones by pointing to bitcoin's volatility against fiat currencies as a barrier to wider adoption. De Haan concluded, "Ordinary people benefit from price stability and that is the most important mandate for central banks."
Many people have heard that Obamacare's enrollment numbers fell short of initial projections made by the CBO in 2010. However, the CBO also predicted that Obamacare would result in 8 million people losing private insurance by 2016. This did not occur. Instead, due to the individual and employer mandates within Obamacare, total private coverage increased by over 16 million through mid-2015. This is evident in the chart, which shows private coverage rising from around 61% of the population to 66% in a single year. While the economic recovery likely played a role, the only significant change in the fourth quarter of 2013 was the implementation of Obamacare. Overall, while Obamacare may have missed the CBO's enrollment target by approximately 7 million, it exceeded their projection for private insurance by 24 million. This is positive news as employer-sponsored insurance is generally better coverage and less costly for taxpayers. This success story deserves more attention.
Two Marines are urging Congress to allow more Afghan interpreters to obtain visas. They fear for the interpreters' safety as the Taliban threatens them after the U.S. withdrawal from Afghanistan. Marine Sgt. Andrew Biggio and Marine platoon Sgt. Peter Boisvert worked alongside Afghan interpreters and believe they saved lives. A December 31st deadline looms for interpreters to apply for 1,000 new visas. Rep. James McGovern is pushing to extend the deadline and increase the number of visas available. Since 2009, approximately 13,000 visas have been issued to Afghan nationals. McGovern argues that the U.S. has a moral obligation to protect these individuals, as they are targets of the Taliban. His amendment is part of the National Defense Authorization Act for 2015, currently under consideration by the Senate. One Afghan interpreter, who recently arrived in Boston, was fortunate to secure a visa with the help of the Marines. He fears for his family's safety back in Afghanistan and reports that interpreters who are captured by the Taliban are often killed. Another interpreter he knows was beheaded while traveling with his family. This interpreter spent three years obtaining his visa and sold his car to afford his plane ticket to the U.S. Biggio emphasizes that Afghan people appreciated the presence of coalition forces and that his interpreter dedicated his life to protecting them. McGovern stresses the urgency of addressing this issue and highlights that there are 9,000 individuals seeking visas, while only 4,000 were granted this year. He believes that extending the deadline and increasing the number of visas is a crucial step in preventing the deaths of interpreters and their families.

Shares initially declined by as much as 5% before experiencing a slight recovery. Germany's Bild newspaper reported that the country's regulators discovered evidence of emissions-cheating software in Fiat vehicles. The newspaper stated that the German Federal Motor Vehicle Office (KBA) found software in a tested Fiat car that deactivated emissions control technology after 22 minutes, two minutes past the standard test duration. Bild claimed the KBA had informed both the European Commission and Italian authorities of its findings. Fiat Chrysler has yet to respond to the allegations. The company also canceled a planned meeting with German authorities last week, citing Italy's responsibility for testing. German Transport Minister Alexander Dobrindt criticized Fiat's uncooperative stance, calling it "totally incomprehensible."

The Royal College of Nursing (RCN) believes a specific law is needed to address verbal and physical abuse against nurses treating the public. Daily, 193 attacks on UK healthcare workers are reported, with a 4% increase in physical assaults against healthcare workers in England between 2014/15 and 2015/16, according to NHS Protect. An RCN survey revealed that 56% of members had experienced physical or verbal abuse from patients, and 63% from patients' relatives or the public. A parliamentary debate will now take place for MPs to discuss making it a criminal offence to attack a public healthcare professional. This debate was triggered by a petition signed by over 116,000 people, following a campaign led by LBC radio presenter Nick Ferrari. The hope is that a specific law will deter individuals from attacking nurses. These incidents cost the health service an estimated £69 million annually due to staff absence, decreased productivity, and increased security needs. The Government stated that assaults against NHS staff are unacceptable and already criminalised, with more severe penalties if the victim serves the public. They believe a new offence would not provide additional protection. Kathryn Yates, RCN Professional Lead for Primary and Community Care, expressed ongoing concern for the safety of lone-working nurses in the community. She highlighted that little has changed since 2015, when half of surveyed community nurses felt vulnerable at work. Nearly half also reported that risks had worsened due to NHS cost-cutting. Increasing caseloads and staff shortages are leading to more nurses working alone. Some local councils have cut community nursing budgets by 50%, resulting in a 42% reduction in the number of district nurses. Shortages of general practice nurses and nurse practitioners further contribute to the workforce crisis in general practice. This staff shortage and the increased need for out-of-hours working pose significant risks.
Over 500 school districts nationwide have committed to correctly pronouncing every student's name. This pledge stems from leftist education groups who view mispronouncing names as a "microaggression." This concept of "microaggressions" encompasses everyday words and actions that radical leftists deem worthy of anger or frustration. The initiative to pronounce every student's name accurately originated with a campaign launched last year by the National Association for Bilingual Education and the Santa Clara, California, school district. The campaign, "My Name, My Identity: A Declaration of Self," suggests that mispronounced names cause "anxiety and resentment" in students. The campaign's pledge encourages teachers to state their names and promise to "show respect to others' names and identities in schools by pronouncing students' names correctly," as well as share name stories on social media and celebrate diversity. Rita Kohli, an education professor at the University of California, Riverside, recently stated that mispronouncing a student's name can hinder their learning process. Kohli, writing in NEA Today, the publication of the National Education Association, emphasized the significance of names to families and cultures. She argued that mispronunciation disrespects both the student and their background. A 2014 article on "Cult of Pedagogy" claimed that mispronouncing names is akin to "mutilating someone's name" and a form of "bigotry." The author, Jennifer Gonzalez, stated that while students may not know the term "microaggression," they likely feel marginalized when their names are mispronounced.
Romanian Prime Minister Sorin Grindeanu announced on Saturday that the government intends to repeal the controversial corruption decree that has sparked the country's largest protests since the 1989 fall of dictator Nicolae Ceausescu. Grindeanu stated at a news conference, "Tomorrow we will hold a government meeting to repeal this decree. I do not want to divide Romania. It cannot be divided in two." He added that he would call an extraordinary Cabinet meeting on Sunday to find a legal way to prevent the decree from taking effect. An estimated 120,000 people protested in Romania on Saturday for the fifth consecutive day against the decree, which decriminalized certain official misconduct offenses. Demonstrators marched through Bucharest to the parliament building, forming a human chain. On Friday, between 200,000 and 250,000 people demonstrated nationwide. The government's decision to withdraw the decree was anticipated, as the leader of Grindeanu's Social Democrats (PSD) had earlier indicated the possibility. Grindeanu emphasized that the decree would not take effect. Calin Tariceanu, head of the ALDE party, a junior coalition partner of the PSD, stated on Romanian TV that the move was made because "we have nothing to hide." Romania's Constitutional Court is expected to rule next week on the legality of the decree. The decree, which decriminalized abuse-of-power offenses for amounts below 200,000 lei ($47,500; 44,200 euros) and narrowed the definition of conflict of interest, was met with strong criticism. Critics argue that the decree's true purpose was to shield thousands of politicians and officials involved in anti-corruption investigations, many of whom are members of the PSD. The PSD returned to power in December after winning elections with promises to raise salaries and pensions in Romania, the European Union's second-poorest country.

The collectors’ market for misprinted money is thriving. The complex minting process can lead to a variety of errors, making each misprint unique and valuable. One example is a $1 bill with an inverted back, misaligned by 25%. This error increases the bill's value to $1,053, a 105,200% increase. Another example is a $1 bill with a foldover, making it appear as if George Washington is blowing his nose. This foldover increases the bill's value to $760.5, a 75,950% increase. A $20 bill with a shifted star near Andrew Jackson's head, along with shifted serial numbers and seals, is valued at $643, a 3,115% increase. A $1 bill with multiple gutter folds, resembling lines from a laser show, is valued at $175.5, a 17,450% increase. A $10 bill missing the face printing, while retaining other features, is valued at $351, a 3,050% increase. A $20 bill missing the back printing is valued at $555.75, a 2,678.75% increase. Finally, a $10 bill with an ink smear, resembling a squid attack, is valued at $438.75, a 4,287.5% increase.

The Arizona Coyotes traded defenseman Anthony DeAngelo and the seventh overall pick in the draft to the New York Rangers for center Derek Stepan and goalie Antti Raanta. The Coyotes have undergone significant changes since Andrew Barroway became the sole owner, including trading away goalie Mike Smith, parting ways with coach Dave Tippett, and not offering captain Shane Doan a contract. They also acquired defenseman Niklas Hjalmarsson from Chicago earlier on Friday. Stepan, 27, had 17 goals and 38 assists in 81 games with the Rangers last season. Arizona has lacked a top-line center for several years and hopes Stepan can fill that role. "We are thrilled to acquire Derek," Coyotes GM John Chayka said. "Our organization has been searching for a true number one center for over a decade and we are confident that he can be that for us." Raanta had a 16-8-2 record with four shutouts and a 2.26 goals-against average in 30 games as Henrik Lundqvist's backup. He will be Arizona's starting goalie after Smith was traded to Calgary. "Antti is a highly skilled, athletic goaltender," Chayka said. "We're confident that he can emerge as an excellent starting goalie for us." DeAngelo had five goals and nine assists in 39 games last season, his first in the NHL. The Rangers now have the seventh and 21st picks as GM Jeff Gorton seeks the right combination of players.
Haribo, a German gummy maker, announced it will open its first U.S. factory in Kenosha County, Wisconsin. The factory, scheduled to open in 2020, will create 400 jobs and represents one of the largest investments from a foreign company in Wisconsin's history. Wisconsin Governor Scott Walker welcomed Haribo, citing the state's business-friendly environment and strong fiscal management. Construction is expected to begin next year with a capital investment of $242 million in the first phase. The 500,000 square foot facility will be the third-largest confectionery plant in the U.S. Haribo, known for its Gold-Bears gummy bears, also produces gummy coca cola bottles, licorice, sour peaches, and other treats. The family-run company employs nearly 7,000 people globally and has manufacturing facilities in 10 countries. Kenosha County Executive Jim Kreuser expressed enthusiasm for the plant's impact on the county and state.

Volkswagen is facing a new crisis after discovering "irregularities" in the carbon dioxide emissions of approximately 800,000 cars in Europe, potentially affecting many more globally. This issue follows the company's previous scandal involving manipulated nitrous oxide (NOx) emissions. While methods exist to reduce NOx, Volkswagen opted for a cheaper but ultimately unsustainable approach: cheating on emissions tests. Now, Volkswagen reports that mainly 1.4-liter diesel engines in models like the Polo and Golf, along with some Audi, Seat, and Skoda vehicles, may have issues with CO2 emissions. Volkswagen claims to have uncovered these inconsistencies during investigations into the "defeat device" used to lower NOx emissions during certification. It appears that these cars produced significantly less CO2 during testing than in real-world conditions. The company has set aside €2 billion to address this issue, four times the amount allocated for the NOx scandal. The CO2 irregularities could be more damaging than the NOx scandal because CO2 emissions are directly linked to fuel consumption. If Volkswagen manipulated data to show higher mileage and lower emissions than reality, it faces substantial regulatory fines. The potential cost for Volkswagen could reach tens of billions of euros if the issue affects more than 800,000 vehicles. Consequently, Volkswagen's share price has plummeted by approximately 10 percent since the announcement.
If you suspect you are being drained by a succubus or incubus, the first step is to identify the individual. Then, sever all contact by removing pictures, phone numbers, and anything else that might maintain a link. Blessings or spells can help disconnect you from them on the astral plane. However, succubi and incubi often have specific preferences and may not easily relinquish their chosen target. Because we constantly exchange energy with those we interact with, they could target your close friends and family. They might drain their energy, as it resembles yours, and maintain a connection to you by constantly talking about you. Compliments can be a tool to encourage this, especially with mothers. This stirs emotions, allowing them to feed. Eventually, the individual they are targeting needs to return to "refuel," requiring more time with you. This intermediary can sometimes lead the person to become an energy vampire or succubus themselves. Removing a succubus or incubus is challenging, as they often dislike leaving an unfinished meal. The most effective method is to create a negative impression, requiring the assistance of a skilled spiritual advisor like a Native American Shaman, Wiccan Priestess, or Voodoo Doctor. Destroying a succubus or incubus is extremely difficult and can leave the person a depressed, empty shell. It's rare to restore someone to their original state after a succubus has drained them. The only chance of saving someone is if you intervene while they are being drained, not after the damage is done.
Wine-tasting trips to Washington state can be booked now, or you could wait a few decades. By then, rising temperatures might have added nearly 80,000 square miles of suitable vineyard land to the Pacific Northwest, land currently inhabited by grizzly bears and wolves. However, most grape varieties in 60 percent of California's current wine-growing regions will face tougher conditions, according to biologist Lee Hannah of Conservation International. Hannah used three methods to assess wine grape suitability and mapped the global impact of climate change on viticulture. Mediterranean Europe, South Africa, and Australia will also struggle, while higher latitudes and elevations, including parts of New Zealand and northern Europe, will benefit. Vineyards can adapt through methods like shadier trellising and planting heat-tolerant varietals, potentially leading to an increase in Zinfandel production.

Chanel Dubofsky, a 37-year-old writer who lives in both New York and Massachusetts, believes she has always been a high achiever. This tendency began when her mother was diagnosed with cancer when Dubofsky was seven years old, and intensified when her mother relapsed twice, at ages thirteen and seventeen. Dubofsky remembers experiencing intense headaches in middle school that persisted until she finished her homework. She says her high school friends encouraged this work-driven lifestyle, engaging in a competition about who could get the least amount of sleep. Dubofsky adds that her workaholism became particularly intense during the college semester her mother passed away. Even after her mother's death, Dubofsky enrolled in 20 credit hours of classes, typical for most students to take 12 or 15, staying up all night to complete assignments and sleeping until noon. She managed to earn As in all her classes, including morning classes she often skipped due to exhaustion, only later informing her professors about her recent loss. "I knew there were expectations for how someone grieving was supposed to act, and I wasn't fulfilling any of them." Research on mental health indicates that grief can manifest in various ways, such as anger, guilt, depression, or decreased productivity, and there is no single "right" way to grieve. However, unhealthy responses to grief include making significant life changes (like moving or quitting a job) before processing the grief, or attempting to minimize or avoid one's feelings. While using work tasks to cope with stress can be beneficial in moderation, especially if it provides purpose and meaning, individuals with traumatic stress or PTSD may be more likely to channel or avoid unresolved feelings through overwork, often becoming so absorbed in their work that they neglect physical discomfort or pain. Bryan Robinson, a psychotherapist and author of Chained To The Desk, explains this tendency. A 29-year-old nonprofit worker in New York, who requested anonymity to protect her privacy, identifies as a trauma survivor and workaholic. "For me, working constantly and staying busy is a way to avoid confronting my emotions," she shares. Rebekah Reysen, a researcher at The University of Mississippi who co-authored an article on workaholism in teachers, suggests that overworking can be a form of escape: "People can run toward something as they run away from something else." Nancy, a New York City-based journalist and documentary filmmaker, observed her tendency towards overwork most prominently when collaborating with an abusive partner on a project
Eddie Gossage, president of Texas Motor Speedway, expressed territoriality regarding NASCAR races in Texas after Circuit of the Americas (COTA) showed interest in hosting the series. COTA chairman Bobby Epstein recently stated his desire to host NASCAR races, despite COTA being a road course, unlike TMS's oval track, and located three and a half to four hours away. However, NASCAR dates in Texas are protected under a regional clause in TMS's five-year contract with the series, according to Autoweek. Gossage emphasized TMS's long-standing relationship with NASCAR, stating that COTA was "20 years too late" in expressing interest. This territoriality has drawn criticism, as the distance between TMS and COTA is comparable to the distance between other nearby tracks like Homestead-Miami and Daytona or Martinsville and Bristol. While scheduling considerations are important, NASCAR only races at TMS twice a year, allowing for potential road course dates at COTA without significant overlap. Moreover, road course races attract new fans who might be interested in attending TMS events. Gossage's emphasis on TMS's long history with NASCAR suggests that the track should be confident in its position and not view COTA as a threat. Given their long-standing relationship, TMS should not be concerned about COTA hosting NASCAR races, especially when scheduled strategically.
Three years after the earthquake, tsunami, and nuclear disaster that devastated Japan, over 2,600 people are still missing. Search efforts continue along the northeastern coastline, where the majority of the 15,884 lives lost were claimed by the tsunami on March 11, 2011. As Japan prepares to commemorate the third anniversary of the disaster, the nation's worst peacetime tragedy, the National Police Agency reports that 2,636 people remain officially missing, primarily in the hardest-hit prefectures of Miyagi, Iwate, and Fukushima. The remains of 98 individuals have yet to be identified. While many disaster-affected communities appear to have returned to a semblance of normalcy, with debris cleared and businesses reopened, the challenges faced by families who have lost loved ones remain significant. Monthly searches are conducted by police, maritime safety personnel, and volunteers at the request of relatives of the missing. Yasuo Takamatsu, a 57-year-old bus driver from Onagawa, Miyagi prefecture, is determined to find his wife, Yuko, who disappeared in the tsunami. Takamatsu recently learned to scuba dive in hopes of searching the seabed for any sign of his wife. Yuko, a 47-year-old bank worker, was one of 250 people still missing from Onagawa, which suffered over 800 deaths from the 65-foot tsunami. Takamatsu recalled his wife's last text message, sent shortly after the earthquake: "I want to go home." Another unsent message found on her phone read "Tsunami huge." "That was the last message from her," Takamatsu said. "I feel terrible thinking she is still out there. I want to bring her home as soon as possible."
Analysts predict gas prices could drop below $3 per gallon by fall, citing recent oil price declines. Oil prices have fallen 6 percent in the last month and $0.49 since their April peak. Tom Kloza, an oil market expert, anticipates prices to reach below $3 by Halloween and certainly by Thanksgiving. This downward trend coincides with the peak summer driving season. AAA forecasts 42.3 million Americans will travel 50 miles or more over the Fourth of July weekend, a nearly 5 percent increase from last year, with 35 million opting for car travel. The current national average gas price is $3.45 per gallon, according to AAA's daily fuel gauge report. Crude oil prices have fallen to $78 a barrel, the lowest point since late winter, according to West Texas Intermediate market reports.

Marvel Comics has canceled the Luke Cage comic book series. Writer David F. Walker confirmed that February’s issue #170 will be the final one. Walker stated that the series was canceled due to low sales, attributing the issue to a general decline in comic book readership. He noted that only four of the top ten best-selling comics in November sold over 100,000 units. Luke Cage is the sixth series to be canceled by Marvel recently, joining Generation X, Hawkeye, Iceman, Jean Grey, and The Unbelievable Gwenpool. Secret Warriors and Defenders have also been removed from Marvel's March 2018 solicitations, though their cancellations have not been officially confirmed. Writer Matthew Rosenberg stated that some series end to make way for new ones, characterizing it as a natural cycle. The Mighty Captain Marvel was also absent from the March solicitations, but writer Margeret Stohl assured fans that the series is not canceled. Mark Waid similarly addressed concerns about Champions potentially being canceled after the March solicitations included a synopsis suggesting a team breakup. Waid confirmed that the book is not ending. Guardians of the Galaxy was recently canceled, but this cancellation will lead to the Infinity Countdown series.
A survey by pro-independence group Yes Scotland found that less than half of Scots feel sufficiently informed about independence and the 2014 referendum. The survey, which polled over 1,000 people in April, revealed that 46% of respondents believe they have enough knowledge about the issues. Of those who feel informed, 47% plan to vote yes, while 50% plan to vote no. The survey also found that 74% of people are interested in the independence debate, compared to 10% who are not. Additionally, 75% said they are "very likely" to vote, and another 13% said they are "quite likely" to vote. Blair Jenkins, chief executive of Yes Scotland, stated that the surveys, along with other research, indicate that increased knowledge about independence leads to greater support for it. He expressed confidence that if they continue to effectively communicate their message, they have a strong chance of success. Jenkins also noted that with just over a year until the referendum, they are currently running neck-and-neck with the No campaign among informed voters.

AMD has changed the codename for its next-generation high-end Radeon GPU family from "Pirate Islands" to "Caribbean Islands." Rumors suggest these cards will be released sometime between June and August. This information comes from Fudzilla, which claims its sources are reliable. However, release dates are often difficult to predict, so it's wise to be cautious about this timeframe. AMD might accelerate the production of "Caribbean Islands" GPUs if Nvidia releases its GeForce GTX 990 early in 2015. The GTX 990, a successor to the powerful GTX 980, could widen the performance gap between Nvidia and AMD, potentially forcing AMD to act sooner. AMD is likely to use a 20-nanometer fabrication process for the next-generation Radeon R9 290 and 295X2, but a jump to 16-nanometer FinFET technology is possible given the potential late release date. AMD often hosts grand events in the Caribbean to introduce new graphics processing units, as it did for the "Hawaii" GPU. Several Caribbean islands have been used as codenames for Radeon products in the past, such as "Cayman XT" for the HD 6970 and "Antilles XT" for the Radeon HD 6990.
After a year of closed testing on private roads, Google is releasing its self-driving bubble car prototype onto California's public roads this summer. A few prototypes will be driving around Mountain View with a human operator present, along with a removable steering wheel, accelerator, and brake pedal for manual control if needed. The cars will be limited to 25 mph. Google is drawing on its experience with its self-driving Lexus RX450h SUV fleet, which has accumulated nearly a million autonomous miles on California roads since September 2015. The Lexus fleet currently drives about 10,000 miles per week, equivalent to "75 years of typical American adult driving experience" according to Google. The bubble cars will utilize the software and experience gained from the Lexus fleet during their public testing. Google views this as a necessary step towards fully autonomous driving and a significant one for public perception and acceptance. Public testing will help alleviate concerns about the safety of self-driving cars, despite evidence to the contrary. Following recent reports of accidents involving self-driving cars in California, Google emphasized that out of 11 minor accidents involving their vehicles over 1.7 million miles of autonomous and manual driving, none were caused by the self-driving system.

Israel's divorce rate decreased by 2% in 2014, according to the Chief Rabbinate of Israel. The Rabbinate reported 11,023 divorces in 2014, down from 11,249 in 2013, based on cases handled by religious courts nationwide. Despite the overall decline, divorces increased in both Jerusalem and Tel Aviv, which have consistently ranked first and second in recent years. Jerusalem led with 874 divorces in 2014, up from 733 in 2013. Tel Aviv followed with 779 divorces in 2014, compared to 678 in 2013. Haifa came in third with 629 divorces, followed by Petach Tikvah with 439. Azor had the lowest number at 29. In 2014, the Rabbinate system processed 89,551 divorce cases, a decrease from 90,237 in 2013. Of these cases, 7,541 involved couples seeking divorce, 969 related to marital disputes, with only 2.6% approved by the Rabbinate. There were 3,437 cases concerning alimony, 3,341 for inheritance matters, and 2,501 for child custody. The unit responsible for assisting agunos (women whose husbands refuse to grant a divorce) successfully secured a get for 184 women in 2014, compared to 159 in 2013. Religious courts imposed sanctions on 40 recalcitrant husbands, with 184 husbands fleeing the country. Sanctions include asset freezes, travel restrictions, and limitations on employment and driver's licenses. Imprisoned recalcitrant husbands face harsher conditions than convicted criminals. The courts issued 16 arrest warrants for recalcitrant husbands in 2014, down from 19 in 2013, and issued protective orders in 152 cases to protect wives from spousal violence, an increase from 140 in 2013. The Chief Rabbinate reported a 40-day reduction in the time it takes to address cases, with an average closure time of 56 days
A German village is using mobile phones as remote controls for streetlights to reduce energy costs and emissions. Residents in Dörentrup, a village 60 miles from Hanover, can turn on streetlights as needed by making a phone call. The village has successfully tested the "Dial4Light" system on several streets for a year and plans to expand it throughout the village and beyond. All 9,000 residents can register for the free system. They call a number and enter a code displayed on a lamp post, triggering the lights to turn on within seconds. The lights stay on for up to 15 minutes before automatically switching off. The mayor of Dörentrup, Friedrich Ehlert, stated that the primary goal is to save money, but it also raises awareness about energy consumption. The project originated when the village council, facing financial difficulties, turned off streetlights at night. A resident proposed a system to allow on-demand lighting, which the council developed with experts and patented. The Dial4Light system is expected to reduce the village's annual carbon dioxide emissions by nearly 20 tonnes, equivalent to the emissions of 11 four-person households. International interest in the system has prompted the Lemgo county council to launch Dial4Light globally later this month.
Former US Vice President Dick Cheney strongly defended the use of torture by American interrogators against terrorist suspects during the George W. Bush administration. Catch.com, a popular multi-platform note-taking service, will cease operations on August 30th. The company notified users today, offering them the option to export their notes before the service is discontinued. The associated Android app, Catch Notes, is also expected to become unusable on that date. Catch had millions of users, and its Android app garnered between 5 and 10 million installs on the Play Store. The company offered little explanation for the closure, only stating that it will be pursuing a "different direction." With growing competition from services like Google Keep and Evernote, the note-taking market has become increasingly crowded. It's possible Catch determined it couldn't keep pace.

The US military has acknowledged using depleted uranium (DU) ammunition in Syria in late 2015 during strikes against supposed Daesh (ISIL) positions. Reports indicate the military fired thousands of rounds containing mutagenic depleted uranium. Weapons experts consider the use of DU a war crime, a crime against humanity, and an act of genocide due to its harmful effects on civilians. Exposure to DU can lead to genetic damage, birth defects, cancer, immune system disorders, and other serious health problems. A US Central Command (CENTCOM) spokesman confirmed the use of DU, stating that a combination of DU and High Explosive Incendiary rounds was employed to increase the likelihood of destroying Daesh's truck fleet used for transporting oil. He revealed that 5,265 armor-piercing 30mm DU rounds were fired on November 16 and November 22, 2015, destroying approximately 250 vehicles. This admission contradicts previous statements from the US military, which had pledged not to use DU weapons in Iraq and Syria. In March 2015, a US-led coalition spokesman claimed that coalition aircraft were not and would not use DU munitions in Iraq or Syria. The Pentagon echoed this stance, stating that DU weapons were not and would not be used against Daesh because the group lacked the armored vehicles the weapons were designed to penetrate. The use of DU weapons in Iraq during the 2003 invasion resulted in widespread health concerns. The Iraqi government expressed deep concern over the harmful effects of DU, which was used extensively in densely populated areas. A 2014 UN report on DU weapons stated that they pose a danger to humans and the environment, calling for in-depth studies on their effects. Iraqi doctors and international health scientists have linked DU contamination to a surge in previously unseen diseases, including kidney, lung, and liver ailments, as well as immune system collapse. They also attribute the rise in leukemia, renal, and anemia cases, particularly among children, to DU contamination.

This book offers a philosophical, ideological, and computational analysis of the BioShock video game franchise, focusing on systems, decisions, and propaganda. The author uses BioShock to explore questions of choice, causality, and decision-making both within and outside the game. The book avoids superficial assessments of the game's aesthetics and cultural impact, instead providing a critical examination of the ideology of decision in contemporary society. It argues that decision-making is not solely an individual capacity but is shaped by systems and collectives. BioShock serves as a lens through which to understand the complexities of forced choice, decision-making structures, and the political landscape. The book highlights how individual agency is often predetermined by external forces and systems, with decisions becoming automated, algorithmic, and systemic.

Puya raimondii, also known as the queen of the Andes, titanka, or puya de Raimondi, is the largest bromeliad species, growing up to 15 meters tall. Native to the high Andes of Bolivia and Peru, it was first described scientifically in 1830 by Alcide d'Orbigny, who found it in Bolivia at an altitude of 3960 meters. However, the plants he observed were immature and not flowering, preventing a taxonomic classification. The species name honors the 19th-century Italian scientist Antonio Raimondi, who discovered it in Peru and published it as Pourretia gigantea in 1874. In 1928, the name was changed to Puya raimondii by Hermann Harms due to an existing species with the name Puya gigantea. The queen of the Andes has a trunk that can reach 5 meters tall, topped with a rosette of leaves up to 1.25 meters long, with spines reaching 1 centimeter long. Its inflorescence can grow 4 to 8 meters tall, and the entire plant can reach 15 meters. A single plant can produce between 8,000 and 20,000 flowers over a 3-month period. Its reproductive cycle lasts approximately 80 years, although a plant grown near sea level in California bloomed after only 28 years. Found in the Andes of Bolivia and Peru, between 3000 and 4800 meters elevation, P. raimondii prefers shrubby, rocky slopes. It seems to be highly specialized to its environment, growing in small areas even when surrounding terrain appears suitable, resulting in a patchy distribution. Interestingly, it has thrived at near sea level in a temperate climate. The IUCN classifies P. raimondii as an endangered species. Threats to its survival include human-caused fires, climate change, and declining genetic diversity.

Regardless, Sher deserves credit for guiding these talented performers, including the outstanding supporting cast, to deliver such compelling performances.

The Annual Days of Yore festival, organized by the Mountain View Arts Society, takes place in Rosebud Park, Didsbury, Alberta. Over two days, visitors can witness historical reenactments featuring dozens of participants, exciting sword fights, impressive cannons, and live combat demonstrations. Explore the reenactment village, "Stroll Through the Ages," and encounter groups like the Sons of Fenrir Vikings and the Dragons Own Medieval Combat Group. The festival offers a full day of battles, music with pipers and fifers, sing-alongs, and storytelling. Artisan Alley showcases heritage arts and traditional crafts, adding to the authentic atmosphere. This unique festival allows visitors to interact with soldiers from various historical periods, including Vikings, King Arthur's Knights, Hudson's Bay Company fur traders, and World War commanders. Feel free to dress up in your favorite period costume and join the fun, perhaps even playing croquet with Queen Victoria's friends. Free parking is available, and the festival is wheelchair and stroller accessible. Food trucks will be on site. Only service dogs are permitted.
Verizon has been transparent about its goal of replacing traditional voice calls with Voice over LTE (VoLTE). They conducted test calls in early 2011 using the LG Revolution and initially planned to launch the service commercially in 2012. An analyst at Northland Capital Markets suggests that Verizon already has two trial VoLTE markets operational and aims for a national rollout in 2013. This doesn't rule out a limited commercial launch this year, as Verizon might introduce the service gradually in different markets. The 2013 nationwide availability aligns with Verizon's plan to upgrade its entire 3G network with LTE next year. VoLTE is significant because LTE is entirely IP-based, meaning legacy calling services cannot be deployed on LTE without reverting to CDMA or GSM. VoLTE is essential for carriers to eventually eliminate 2G and 3G networks and repurpose their spectrum, although this transition will take time.

Blind gamers often feel excluded, as evidenced by Brandon Cole's experience when he suggested accessibility improvements for THQ's Smackdown games and received a form letter focused on graphics. Adding to the challenge, some EverQuest II characters are literally invisible. However, one visually impaired gamer, Alexander Stern, has taken legal action against Sony, Sony Online Entertainment, and Sony Computer Entertainment America. Stern's lawsuit alleges that Sony violates the Americans with Disabilities Act by not providing accessibility features for visually impaired gamers. The suit claims that Sony ignored repeated requests for modifications to make its games more accessible. It specifically requests visual cues to aid navigation for gamers with visual processing disabilities, citing examples like Blizzard Entertainment's World of Warcraft, which allows third-party mods for accessibility aids, and Pin Interactive's Terraformers, which features high-contrast graphics, an audio compass, and voice-over item descriptions. Stern also argues that Sony's actions have caused him financial losses because he cannot participate in the company's official auction site for in-game items. The lawsuit states that Sony has intentionally designed inaccessible products and failed to address the issue despite repeated notifications. Sony and Sony Online Entertainment declined to comment on the ongoing litigation.
Microsoft's decision to use AMD chips in its next Xbox suggests it will not support Xbox 360 games, according to a new report. The unnamed console, reportedly called "Xbox", will use a version of AMD Jaguar technology. The shift to x86 PC architecture aims to simplify and reduce the cost of game development for companies familiar with the standard. However, this move effectively ends backwards compatibility with current-generation games, Bloomberg reported. Sony faces a similar challenge with the PlayStation 4 but has a solution: streaming older games using its Gaikai technology. Backwards compatibility was already considered unlikely. Analyst Paul Thurrott recently revealed Microsoft's plan to release a $99 Xbox 360 model alongside the new console, indicating a lack of backwards compatibility. Digital Foundry's Rich Leadbetter believes that while achieving backwards compatibility with completely different hardware configurations is difficult, Microsoft managed to make a significant number of Xbox games playable on the Xbox 360 despite the change in architecture. However, the time and resources needed to ensure flawless compatibility for a large library of games make it unlikely for the new Xbox. Sources claim Microsoft will announce the new Xbox on May 21st, with a November launch and a price of $500, or $300 with a subscription.

Slovenia held a referendum today on same-sex marriage and related issues. The vote aimed to overturn a parliamentary law that aimed to align Slovenia with other more progressive European Union member states. Despite expectations of failure due to stringent conditions, Slovenians voted overwhelmingly to reject the law. Andrej Turjaški, our correspondent in Slovenia, reports: "God has granted us a miracle." The Slovenian people rejected the law proposed by the parliamentary majority, which would have granted marriage equality to homosexuals and allowed them to adopt children and teach gender theory in schools. Voter turnout exceeded 36%, with 63% voting against the law. This surpassed the required threshold of 20% of registered voters for the law to be rejected, a significant achievement given that typical referendum turnout is around 10%.

"The Art of Shin Godzilla" is a large art book showcasing Hideaki Anno's (known for "Evangelion") take on the iconic monster. This film significantly diverges from previous Japanese and American Godzilla movies, making it my favorite Japanese version to date. Due to the book's substantial size (560 pages), I'm reviewing it in two parts. This first part covers the contents and concept art. Weighing 3.5 kg, the two-volume set arrives in a white cardboard box with the books nestled in a sturdy black slipcase. Included are two flyers: one for the upcoming Blu-ray release and another for a high-quality Godzilla figure replica. The slipcase features the film's Japanese title embossed on it. The first volume showcases a towering Godzilla image, while the second volume contains the film's Japanese screenplay, likely only interesting to readers fluent in the language. The main volume includes two foldout posters illustrated by Uruta Kenji (known for his art book "Future") and Mahiro Maeda, the film's creature designer (director of "Blue Submarine"). The book begins with Mahiro Maeda's concept art and Godzilla designs. The sketches and image boards are detailed and high quality, offering a glimpse into the various Godzilla iterations before the final design was chosen. Maeda even explored the idea of a self-replicating Godzilla with two heads, but Toho Studios ultimately rejected it. Some of the most intriguing art pieces depict humanoid creatures emerging from the dormant Godzilla's tail, a detail hinted at in the film's ending but not fully explored. Perhaps these creatures will appear in a potential sequel? Overall, Mahiro Maeda's concept art is exceptional. Part two of the review will be posted in a few weeks. "The Art of Shin Godzilla" details: - Dimensions: 32.5 x 23.3 x 6.6 cm - Hardcover (main volume - 560 pages) + soft cover (screenplay book - 280 pages) - Protective exterior cardboard case and sturdy cardboard slipcase - Full color, in Japanese language

Scientists now have strong evidence to clear the black rat of responsibility for spreading the bubonic plague after nearly eight centuries of blame. Instead, they point to gerbils as the likely culprits. A study published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences challenges the long-held belief that European plague outbreaks were caused by disease-carrying fleas carried by European rat populations. Climate data from the 14th century does not support the idea that warm, dry summers, favorable for rats, were linked to plague outbreaks. Instead, the analysis of 15 tree-ring records, which document yearly weather conditions, shows a connection between plague outbreaks in Europe and wet springs followed by warm summers in central Asia. These conditions are ideal for gerbils, whose fleas carry the plague bacteria. The gerbils and their fleas likely traveled to Europe via the Silk Road, arriving a few years later to cause epidemics. This new research exonerates Europe's black rats from the deaths of over 100 million people during the second plague pandemic, which began with the Black Death in the mid-14th century and continued until the 1800s. It also explains why the disease reappeared periodically rather than remaining constant in Europe as long as rats were present.

A new study by Sleep-Centre.ca and IPG Mediabrands reveals that 62% of Canadians experience sleep problems. Pain is the most common reason for sleeplessness (50%), followed by uncomfortable room temperature (31%). The study also ranked the Top 20 most sleepless cities in Canada, with Kitchener, Ontario, topping the list. Saskatoon and Vancouver tied for second. Toronto residents, despite living in a top 20 sleepless city, report getting the best sleep due to the city's higher proportion of singles. Cities with more children living at home tend to rank higher on the sleep deprivation list. Sleep deprivation costs Canadian companies almost $75 million annually in sick days, as over a quarter of the workforce has called in sick to catch up on sleep. The study surveyed over 3,000 Canadians in April, focusing on demographics and matching the Census of Canada.
Williams Advanced Engineering, the technology division of the Williams Formula 1 team, is expanding its focus on electric vehicles. Known for its Formula One expertise and engineering work for other car manufacturers, Williams Advanced Engineering has also been involved in Formula E, supplying battery packs until McLaren took over the contract last year. Despite this, the company continues to invest in electric vehicle technology, securing $125 million to build an EV battery factory in the UK to produce batteries for Aston Martin’s RapidE. To further demonstrate its capabilities, Williams Advanced Engineering unveiled the FW-EVX, a lightweight electric vehicle platform concept. Similar to Tesla's skateboard-like design, the FW-EVX features innovations in battery pack design, cooling systems, and lightweight structures. One notable innovation is a highly automated process for creating fiber-reinforced suspension components, potentially reducing weight by up to 40% compared to conventional aluminum wishbones. Another innovation involves forming high-strength 3D structures from 2D materials, creating an exoskeleton for a battery module that also contributes to the battery's structural performance. Paul McNamara, Williams Advanced Engineering Technical Director, emphasized the company's commitment to vehicle efficiency and its potential to enhance the competitiveness of future electric vehicles. Craig Wilson, Williams Advanced Engineering Managing Director, stated that the FW-EVX represents a new direction for the company, aiming to facilitate the transition from internal combustion engines to a fully electric future.

It seems all Certificate Authorities (CAs) require personal information for Domain Validation (DV) certificates. Is this due to a policy from major browsers and operating systems that reject CAs without such requirements? Or are there other reasons for the lack of privacy-focused CAs? I want to replace my self-signed SSL certificate with one from a trusted CA for my personal web server. While I understand that some use cases necessitate thorough identity checks, I only need a basic DV certificate. I don't want to share personal details irrelevant to end-to-end encryption for personal use, like my home address and phone number. I'm seeking a CA with strong domain validation practices, not one that blindly trusts ownership claims. I just need to prove domain ownership, not provide personal information like my name or address. Why do CAs need this information if I can demonstrate domain ownership? Are they collecting it for potential legal action if something goes wrong with the issued certificate? Is there government pressure to gather this data for law enforcement or intelligence purposes?

Initially, I was intrigued by the prevalence of text-based protocols like HTTP and SMTP on the internet. This seemed odd to me because text is inefficient, and machines, not humans, should interpret protocols. A binary format would save space and be more manageable for software. However, I soon realized the brilliance of this decision. Since everything is text-based, you only need telnet to interact with a server, making exploration easy, especially given the internet's reliance on the Robustness Principle. The ability to explore systems in this way is crucial. Understanding how things work is the first and most important step towards using them effectively. When we don't understand the tools we use, we rely on other tools to hide complexity, simplifying them to the point of being manageable. This trade-off prevents us from creating solutions tailored to the actual problem, instead forcing us to address the simplified problem presented by the abstraction layers. This principle is evident in the HTTP 2.0 draft, which adds unnecessary complexity to the HTTP protocol to satisfy a small group of influential players. Consider Web-based APIs, my current focus. REST, a simple set of principles, embodies the essence of the Web. Used correctly, it is both powerful and easy to understand. In contrast, SOAP, despite being text-based, is significantly more complex than REST because it is designed to be opaque to humans. In my two decades in this field, the only way I know to debug a SOAP transaction is with a hammer and a straitjacket. I'd even prefer an old-school EDI transaction over SOAP any day. The tragedy is that many people fail to grasp the importance of this. Obscurity and excessive abstraction are two of the worst problems in software development. They combine to create a form of willful ignorance that results in poor code. We must resist this trend.

People will constantly tell you what to do throughout your life: stay within boundaries, avoid risks, and play it safe. Some advice comes from a good place, believing you'll succeed by following the norm. Others are simply seeking comfort in knowing they're not alone in their choices. The typical plan involves studying hard, getting good grades, attending a prestigious university, finding a stable job, climbing the career ladder, starting a family, and upgrading your home regularly. These are all valid paths, but they're just one option among many. What if you chose a different route? Imagine dropping out of school and traveling across the country, or dedicating your time to reading and exploring your interests. What if you volunteered in a developing country and encountered people with vastly different perspectives? What if you embraced new experiences, challenged your fears, and embraced the journey, even with its imperfections? You have one life. Live it authentically, but consider alternative paths. There's a vast difference between the conventional life most people choose and the extraordinary lives that are possible. Even if you stumble, you'll have memorable stories to share. Do what you want. I'm not here to dictate your life choices. However, remember that within a century, you and everyone you love will be gone. The worries that consume you now will fade into oblivion. Conformity is a powerful force, but you have the power to break free.

A full-sized replica of the San Salvador, the ship Juan Rodriguez Cabrillo sailed into San Diego Bay in 1542, was moved to a Chula Vista shipyard on Wednesday. Volunteers from the Maritime Museum of San Diego built the vessel over four years at Spanish Landing Park. Originally scheduled for Sunday, the transfer to Marine Group Boat Works was delayed due to bad weather. Dr. Ray Ashley, CEO of the Maritime Museum of San Diego, called the move a "truly monumental day" for the museum, its volunteers, donors, and the region. The San Salvador, which will serve as a floating classroom, is the same size as the original, weighing 150 tons and measuring 92 feet long and 24 feet wide. Materials for the replica came from around the world, including Angelique wood from Suriname, Sapele wood from Nigeria, Southern live oak from Georgia, and Douglas fir from the Pacific Northwest. San Diego Mayor Kevin Faulconer praised the dedication shown in building the ship, calling it an educational opportunity for everyone. The San Salvador will be unveiled to the public over Labor Day weekend at the museum's Festival of Sail.

Six inmates at the work-release site immediately sprang into action, helping the officer. They used his work phone to call 911, removed his gun holster and bulletproof vest to aid his breathing, and turned him over to check on him. As a gesture of gratitude, the officer's family treated the inmates to a free lunch at the park.
Union human resource development minister Smriti Irani stated that quality research can be achieved even with limited funding, refuting claims that the BJP-led government has significantly reduced funding for basic science research. Speaking at the Indian Institute of Science Education and Research (IISER) in Pune to celebrate its 10th anniversary, Irani addressed two misconceptions: that Indian institutes are not ranked highly globally and that substantial financial resources are necessary for excellent research. Irani emphasized that "bigger money does not mean better science," citing an example of a fusion experiment conducted in Bengaluru for Rs 2 lakh, compared to millions of dollars spent on a similar experiment in Orange County. She urged young researchers to focus on producing high-quality science with limited resources. Irani also challenged the notion that Indian institutes lack global competitiveness, arguing that global rankings primarily consider research published in English, neglecting research in Indian languages. She highlighted the development of an Indian ranking system to address this bias, with 3,500 higher education institutions already participating. During the event, Irani launched a centralized website for all six IISERs in India, providing a single point of access for information on subjects, faculty, and ongoing research projects. Irani also recalled Kaustubh Deshpande, an IISER-Pune alumnus who initiated remedial teaching for underprivileged children, inspiring students to mentor underprivileged children and bridge the language gap. She announced that the Global Initiative of Academic Networks (GIAN), which aims to increase the presence of foreign professors in Indian universities, now includes 22 state universities with 400 foreign teachers, and expects 1,000 foreign academics to teach in government institutions this year.
Marijuana spot prices are gradually increasing after a significant drop in the past year. Since 2015, Cannabis Benchmarks has tracked wholesale cannabis prices, observing a decline from an average of $1,953 per pound to a low of $1,487 in 2016. In 2017, the first quarter average rose to $1,613. Jonathan Rubin of Cannabis Benchmarks has noted a pattern in wholesale marijuana prices that resembles a typical commodity market, with prices influenced by supply and harvest schedules. The wholesale marijuana market receives product from three sources with distinct harvest times: indoor growers, who harvest year-round and produce four to six harvests annually; greenhouse growers, who typically have four harvests per year; and outdoor growers, who have a single harvest in the fall. The fall harvest from outdoor growers often leads to price decreases due to the influx of supply. This pattern is evident in the chart provided by Cannabis Benchmarks, which illustrates the fluctuations in wholesale cannabis prices since 2015. Rubin explains that the price spike in August is caused by the depletion of outdoor harvests. Outdoor growers, who have only one harvest per year, tend to sell their entire product in the fall to cover expenses. Subsequently, they reduce sales and allow prices to rise as they gradually introduce more product into the market. Rubin highlights that weather variations and state-specific challenges influence marijuana prices annually. Prices experienced their most significant drop at the beginning of 2016, recovering only during the holidays. According to Rubin, increased supplies from expanded cultivation capacity, improved cultivation techniques, and better supply chain management contributed to a $365 per pound or 19.7% price decline. Prices tend to rise during holiday sales and 420 celebrations. Despite a 17.4% year-over-year decline, prices have increased from an average of $1,487 in the fourth quarter of 2016 to $1,613 in the first quarter of 2017. Oregon's market faced unique challenges, including a testing bottleneck at the end of 2016 and a surge in demand ahead of the legalization of adult use in January. Currently, there are no marijuana trading options like other commodities, preventing farmers from securing prices. However, Tradiv operates a wholesale marketplace that connects cultivators with buyers. Rubin believes the market is currently in a bust cycle. He

A senior NATO naval officer says ISIS is actively seeking to establish a terrorist navy to target cruise ships in the Mediterranean. Vice-Admiral Clive Johnstone told the U.K. Telegraph that the group's expansion in Libya raises serious concerns about its intentions. Johnstone explained that ISIS's control of territory in Libya grants them access to the sea and maritime weaponry, fueling their desire for a maritime arm. He stated, "We know they have ambitions to operate offshore, and they want to have a maritime force." An ISIS navy would pose a significant threat to crucial shipping lanes in the Mediterranean. Johnstone warned that attacks might not be premeditated but could arise from accidental encounters. He expressed his belief that such incidents would likely be unintentional or acts of random terrorism with severe consequences for the West. Johnstone also mentioned that some NATO members are contemplating relocating submarines from the Mediterranean to the North Atlantic to counter Russia's growing influence, potentially creating a window of opportunity for ISIS to carry out seaborne attacks.
The Wall Street Journal has begun selling digital subscriptions through Apple's Newsstand service. This decision means Dow Jones' newspaper joins thousands of other magazines and newspapers that utilize Apple's iTunes store for distribution, giving Apple a percentage of sales and control over customer interactions. Notably, the Journal was one of the last prominent print publishers to not offer app access through Apple. Earlier this year, Time Inc., the previous leading holdout, also joined Newsstand. Apple will retain 30 percent of all subscription revenue generated from in-app sales by the Journal, and it will manage customer billing information, including credit card details and addresses. Like other publications, the Journal can request email addresses from subscribers, a strategy publishers have found effective. While the Journal will continue to sell digital subscriptions, including app access, through its own website, retaining full revenue and customer data from those sales, the Newsstand integration simplifies the process for Apple customers to subscribe using their existing iTunes accounts. Newsstand also enables automatic updates for publications through background downloads, enhancing the reading experience.
Crocodiles and alligators serve as the prototypes for the dragons and hydras found in mythology. Crocodiles are large, amphibious reptiles resembling lizards with tough, scaly skin that is difficult to penetrate except on the underside. They can grow to be 25 to 30 feet long. Ancient Egyptians revered crocodiles, whose primary habitat is the Nile River, though they are also found in rivers across the Indian Ocean. While clumsy on land, crocodiles are swift swimmers that hunt fish, but they also pose a danger to dogs, other animals, and humans who enter the water or approach the bank carelessly. The scientific name for the crocodile is *Lacerta crocodilus*, derived from the Greek word *krokodeilos*, whose origin is unclear. The American crocodile, known as the alligator, gets its name from the Spanish word *El Legarto*, meaning "lizard". The Latin forms are *Lacertus* or *Lacerta*. There is evidence that dragons and crocodiles were once considered interchangeable, as noted by Miss Millington in her work "Heraldry in History, Poetry and Romance". Philip de Thaun, in his "Bestiarus", states that "crocodille signifie diable en ceste vie" (crocodiles signify the devil in this life). Guillim, an old heraldic writer, claims that dragons are so hot that they cannot be cooled by water, but instead, they must breathe in air to refresh themselves, as described in Jeremiah 14:6.
Calgary has won a court appeal against street preacher Artur Pawlowski. In 2007, Pawlowski was ticketed for using an amplifier to preach in a public park without a permit. He initially won his case, with the judge ruling that his religious and expression freedoms were violated. The city appealed the decision, and the acquittal has now been overturned. Justice R.J. Hall stated that the city did not abuse its powers and had to balance public concerns with Pawlowski's wishes. He emphasized the right of Calgary citizens to a healthy environment, including noise control in parks. Pawlowski plans to appeal the decision, stating that it infringes on his freedom of expression. A date for the next appeal has not been set.

FC Barcelona aims to secure a naming rights partner for Camp Nou as soon as possible, with the goal of having the partner's name displayed throughout the stadium by December. This process will not be straightforward, as negotiations typically take over a year. Therefore, the club will need to collaborate with a specialist in this field. American companies are considered leaders in this area, having been involved in stadium sponsorships for nearly a century. FC Barcelona has chosen to work with Van Wagner, an American firm with experience in securing naming rights for 16 clients since 1998, including the New York Jets, New York Giants, and Arsenal. The club's board, while not yet officially announcing the partnership, hopes to have a sponsor in place before the 2016 General Assembly, potentially allowing for its approval at that meeting. Sources indicate that FC Barcelona could earn at least €20 million annually from a naming rights deal, comparable to similar agreements in America and Europe. These deals typically span 20 to 30 years. This substantial revenue is crucial for Barcelona's ambitious project to construct a new Camp Nou and Espai Barça.
The best response to speech you disagree with is more free speech. It is acceptable to exercise your right to free speech to object to the speech of others. This is how the system is supposed to work. In Oregon, the Portland Mayor attempted to prevent a group from assembling because he disagreed with their message (as did I). This is the type of action that weakens the constitutional protections that, in theory, shield us all from government overreach. The government should never be able to silence a citizen for their beliefs. We, the people, have the power of speech to respond to the speech of others. This includes civil debate when appropriate, but also protest speech when necessary. A decade ago in Idaho, Fred Phelps and his "God hates F**s" group wanted to protest a funeral at a Catholic church in Boise. I don't recall the specifics of their motivation. If you're familiar with this group, you know they aim to inflict disgusting, hurtful, hateful rhetoric upon the families and attendees of funerals, amplifying their grief on what is often the worst day of their lives. The Idaho community came together. In addition to non-religious groups, an interfaith coalition of local Christian, Muslim, Jewish, and Buddhist communities organized and formed a human shield around the entire city block surrounding the church. We stood shoulder to shoulder, with our backs to the protesters, shielding those entering the funeral. Facing the people entering the church behind our shield, we held signs of love, hope, and messages of support. This is how you use the First Amendment. It is acceptable for a community to come together and say, "We don't share your values." It is acceptable to organize in opposition. As for emboldening the protesters, the Phelps family left early and went home.
The safety and security of Canadians and their borders are crucial responsibilities of the federal government. Therefore, any policy that weakens security screening for asylum seekers is unacceptable. A Sun Media columnist, Candice Malcolm, reported that the Trudeau government plans to expedite the processing of asylum claims from countries with high approval rates for refugee applications, citing information from former high-ranking officials in the immigration department. This could involve approving certain claims without a hearing, holding shorter hearings, or using a simplified "paper-based" process. While many asylum seekers from the Middle East and Africa, where Canada has historically accepted a significant number of refugees, are genuine, some may pose security threats as terrorists, war criminals, or criminals. Federal officials responded to Malcolm's column by stating that security and criminality screening practices will remain unchanged, but some claims may be approved without hearings. These concerns are not new. The Senate Standing Committee on National Security and Defence expressed similar worries in its June 2015 report, "Vigilance, Accountability and Security at Canada's borders." The committee heard testimony indicating that Canada's immigrant visa application process has become overly reliant on paper submissions, with limited face-to-face interviews. The committee also noted that only a small percentage of immigrants (9-15%) are interviewed by a visa officer before arriving in Canada. Conservative leadership candidate Kellie Leitch has faced criticism for suggesting face-to-face interviews to screen immigrant and refugee applicants for anti-Canadian values. However, former ambassador Martin Collacott told the Senate committee that such interviews are crucial. He emphasized that face-to-face meetings allow visa officers to better assess an applicant's potential security threat, including the presence of extremist views conflicting with Canadian values and objectives. These interviews also provide prospective immigrants with valuable information about integration into Canadian society. Canada is a compassionate nation, but we must remain vigilant about who we admit. Our procedures for determining eligibility must be thorough and well-defined, not arbitrary.
Globalization, digitization, and the internet's rise have increased the importance of intellectual property law in global public policy discussions. Concerns about how intellectual property is managed, licensed, used, and reused are now part of a widespread public conversation, involving far more than just legal experts. Because intellectual property law significantly impacts economics, culture, health, commerce, creativity, and intellectual freedom, there is a growing body of literature on the topic. This literature comes not only from legal scholars and lawyers but also from experts in other fields. In 2012, the Centre for Law, Technology, and Society at the University of Ottawa organized a workshop to bring together academics from diverse disciplines interested in intellectual property law. The workshop aimed to foster interdisciplinary dialogue, encourage collaborative projects, and produce research that examines intellectual property law from multiple perspectives. This book collects the papers presented at that workshop.
Bernie Sanders has gained support from a specific group of American voters, as evidenced by social media trends. While these supporters are too young to vote, they are making their voices heard through the #BabiesForBernie movement. Moms across the U.S. are posting pictures of their children dressed as Sanders, typically featuring young kids wearing glasses and digitally altered gray hair. Siegel was surprised by the widespread popularity of her creation and told CNN that it provided a way to engage with the political conversation while caring for her newborn. The Defense Department scrapped 16 out of 20 C-27A Spartan military transport planes purchased for the Afghan Air Force for $32,000, according to the Special Inspector General for Afghanistan Reconstruction (SIGAR). The planes, based on the Italian-made Aeritalia G222, proved too difficult for the Afghan military to maintain. The Pentagon ended the program in March 2013, after spending at least $486 million. The Defense Logistics Agency destroyed the planes at Kabul International Airport while SIGAR investigated the program's failure. From January to September 2012, the aircraft only flew 234 hours out of the required 4,500 hours. SIGAR is requesting documentation from Air Force Secretary Deborah James regarding the decision to scrap the planes, including why they weren't sold elsewhere and what efforts were made to get a refund. John Sopko, the SIGAR head, also wants to know the final destination of the scrap metal sold to an Afghan company and the fate of parts not scrapped, such as engines and brass components. The remaining four aircraft are at Ramstein Air Force Base in Germany. Sopko has requested advance notice before they are moved or destroyed.
A 32-year-old man from Birmingham named David Ball was sentenced to four years in prison for raiding a supermarket while intoxicated. Ball, wearing only a sheet and Ugg boots, stole cigarettes and led police on a high-speed chase through Warwickshire before crashing into two parked cars. He pleaded guilty to aggravated vehicle taking, burglary, driving under the influence, and other driving offenses. Ball's sentence also included an additional 51 weeks for a previous suspended sentence. Ball had a limited recollection of the events but admitted to the crime after viewing CCTV footage. He had a history of theft dating back to 1990 and had served short prison terms in the past. The court heard that Ball stole his girlfriend's Ugg boots and car before the supermarket raid. His defense attorney argued that the death of Ball's grandmother and the impending arrival of his child contributed to his actions. Ball was disqualified from driving for three years.
During the final week of 2011, amidst several business stories attempting to avoid year-end scrutiny, the debate surrounding the Stop Online Piracy Act (SOPA) emerged as a potential game-changer for 2012. Introduced by Texas Republican Lamar Smith in October, SOPA aimed to strengthen restrictions on online copyright infringement. However, opinions on the proposed legislation proved more nuanced than initially perceived. Internet domain registrar GoDaddy faced the brunt of SOPA controversy. Unlike companies like Google and Facebook, which publicly opposed SOPA's restrictiveness, GoDaddy adopted a pro-SOPA stance, triggering widespread grassroots opposition. A Reddit comment thread in late December, suggesting a "move your domain day" boycott, quickly gained traction, amplifying the anti-GoDaddy sentiment online. At the heart of the dispute lay SOPA's grant of authority to enforcement agencies, particularly the U.S. Department of Justice, to penalize individuals deemed copyright infringers. The proposed boycott and negative publicity prompted GoDaddy CEO Warren Adelman to reverse the company's pro-SOPA position. In a December 23 statement, Adelman emphasized the importance of combating online piracy while acknowledging the need for improvement in the legislation. He stressed the necessity of collaborative efforts among all internet stakeholders and pledged GoDaddy's support for SOPA only if the internet community endorsed it.

Hue Jackson, coach of the Cleveland Browns, is establishing a foundation to combat human trafficking in Cleveland and the surrounding areas. He will officially announce the foundation on July 20th at the Browns facility in Berea. This marks Jackson's first personal foundation, following his recent partnership with Jay Z's Roc Nation. The Hue Jackson Foundation aims to empower children, teenagers, and adult victims of human trafficking through financial support of organizations dedicated to advocating for and representing exploited individuals. Jackson and his wife Michelle, parents of three daughters, created the foundation for reasons deeply important to them, expressing their commitment to supporting non-profit organizations that provide hope and empowerment to victims of sexual exploitation and coercion. Ohio has a high rate of human trafficking cases, according to the National Human Trafficking Resource Center. The foundation will focus on raising awareness, preventing trafficking, and providing healing for victims. Human trafficking involves the exploitation of individuals for sex and labor through violence, coercion, fraud, and debt bondage. Victims can be of any age, gender, race, or socioeconomic background and are found in various settings across the country. Joining Jackson at the announcement event will be Ohio Attorney General Mike DeWine, Renee Jones of the Renee Jones Empowerment Center, and Major Lurlene-Kay M. Johnson of The Salvation Army.

If you watched our recent Volca Vibes episode featuring the Retrokits RK002 active MIDI cable and the Volca Sample, you might have been impressed by the clever microprocessor in the cable that enables chromatic playback of Volca Sample samples. It also allows you to play all 10 samples from a regular keyboard. This is achieved by processing incoming MIDI signals from a standard MIDI keyboard in real time using firmware within the cable's processor. Retrokits has now developed a firmware update for their cable that addresses a limitation of the Volca FM: its lack of velocity response over MIDI. Velocity sensitivity is a crucial element of the DX FM sound. While you can adjust velocity using the velocity fader and record it in a Motion Sequence, Retrokits' new firmware enables real-time velocity mapping through their cable. Firmware updates are applied by uploading a .MID file to the cable, and editing is done using Chrome's Web MIDI in your browser, requiring only a connected MIDI interface with the cable plugged in. And it works.
A program called ‘High Five Friday,’ where police officers greeted students with high-fives at four elementary schools in Northampton, Massachusetts, has been suspended. The program, which began in December after the Northampton Police Department saw it at the International Association of Chiefs of Police, was intended to have officers visit the schools once a month. While the department received support for the program, some community members expressed concerns. At a school committee meeting, concerns were raised that some students might feel uncomfortable with a police presence at the start of their school day. A follow-up meeting with community members revealed concerns for minority students, undocumented children, and children who may have had negative experiences with police. After a meeting between Police Chief Jody Kasper and Northampton School Superintendent Dr. John Provost, it was decided to end the program. The department stated on their Facebook page that while officers enjoyed greeting the students, they couldn't ignore the fact that the program might not be well-received by everyone in the community. Reactions to the cancellation have been mixed. Some community members, like Anisa Schardl, expressed support for the decision, stating that they wouldn't want their children to be scared of going to school. Others have praised the police department for their community outreach efforts. It is unknown when a replacement program will be implemented, but the police department is looking forward to working with the school district to create a new one.
